THere is a kind of Falcon that is called a Merlyn. These Merlyns are very
much like the Haggart falcon in plume, in sear of the foot, in beak and
talons. So as there seems to be no odds or difference at al betwixt them,
save only in the bigness, for she has like demeanure, like plume, and very
like conditions to the Falcon, and in her kind is of like courage, and
there-must be kept as choycely and as daintily as the Falcon.

Assuredly diverse of these Merlyns become passing good hawks and very
skillful, their property by nature is to kill Thrusshes, Larkes, and
Partridges. They flee with greater fierceness and more hotely than any other
hawke of pray. They are of greater pleasure, and full of courage, but a man
must make greater care, and take good heed to them, for they are such busy
and unruly things with their beaks, as diverse times they eat of their own
feet and talons very unnaturally, so as they dye of it. And this is the
reason and true cause, that sildome or never shall you see a mewed or
entermewed Merlyn. For that in the mewe they do spoil themselves, as I have
before declared.

My Italyan Author has these words, both of the shape and in commendation of
the Merlyn.

The Merlyn is (says he) of the shape of a Falcon, less than the Sparowhawke,
more nimble and wight of wing than any other hawke, she does kill all such game
and pray as the Sparowhawke does use to slay, specially small byrds, namely
Larks, Sparowes, and such like, all which she does pursue with exceeding
cruelty and courage.

She is reported to be a hawke of the fiste and not of the lure, albeit a man
may if he will, make her to the lure also. She is a very ventrous hawke and
hardy: by this we may conjecture it: For though she be little bigger than a
Piegon, yet notwithstanding, she will hazard her self to flee the Partridge,
the Quayle, and such other like fowls, more large than her self, and will
pursue them in so cruel manner, as sundry times she follows them, even to the
villages and towns, whether the silly birds do flee for aid and rescue, from
their natural foe the hawke.

The Merlin is the only hawke of al others in whom (as mine Author affirms)
there is no difference betwixt the male and female, but yet by experience we
find it otherwise, for the female is ye larger bird of the two, and more big
than ye other in sight.

Some are of opinion, that Lydos, Hierax, in greek, and Leuis accipiter in
Latin, is our Merlyn of whom we speak, and that those birds of pray whom
Aristotle terms ( Leaves) to our judgment should be the Merlyns, because they
are the less hawks of all others that are to be found.

Of the Hobbye.
depiction of hobby falcon

OF all birds of pray that belong to the Falconers use, I know none less
than the Hobbye unless it be the Merlyn.

The Hobbye is a hawke of the lure, and not of the fist: also she is of the
number of those hawks that are hye fleeing and tower hawks, as the Falcon,
the Laner, and the Sacre be. If a man be disposed to describe the Hobbye, he
can not do better, nor deal more artificially, than to match her for shape
with the Sacre. For in good faith there is but small difference or inequality
betwixt them, save that the Sacre is far the huger bird.

The property of the Hobbie in all countries and regions, where they are
eyreed, or otherwise brought is to soar and flee upon the hountesmen and
Falconers, and so to follow them very watchfully, to the end that when they
spring or put up any small birds, she may sloupe from her wings, and seize on
them as on her pray. And this is so ordinary a hawke, and the practice that I
speak of so general, as there is not the simplest bower or peasant but does
know it. I can make no fitter nor more apt comparison, than to resemble the
frye and small fish of the sea, being had in chase by the huger sort of fish
desirous to devour them: to the small fowls and birds of the air, pursued by
the Hobbye.

For as soon as the silly fish that is chased by the Delphin and such like, do
perceive their safety to be nothing in the Element of water, where, by God and
nature they are allotted to live, eftsone have they their recourse to the air
to save them selves, chosing rather to lie at the mercy of the ravening sea
fowls, soaring upon the water, than to yield them selves in pray to their
natural adversaries the fish. Even so the Hobbies perceiving the huntesmen or
Falconers in the field so hunt the poor Leueret, or flee ye Partridge, do
forthwith accompany them, soring upon them, in hope to encounter some one small
bird or other, whom ye hounds or spanels shall by fortune put up, and spring by
ranging ye field. Then the Larkes and such like small fowls, whose nature is
not to branch or take the tree, but altogether to live upon ye ground, finding
themselves pursued by the hounds and spanels, to begile the^, are enforst to
trust to their wings, and to take the air, and being there, finding themselves
molested by ye Falconers and Hobbies, do make their choice and election to
become a pray rather to the dogs, or seek mercy among the horse legs, and so
to be surprised alive, than to affie in the courtesy of the cruel Hobbies, and
to be taken in their cruel talons, where they are most assured to dye the
death.

The Hobby is so nimble and wight of wing, that she dares encounter the Crow,
and to give souse for souse, and blow for blow with him in the air.

This is a natural and special trick that she does use, espying the
Falconers in the field she does follow them, and attend on them, but it is
but for a certain space, as though in very deed she had her lymites and
bounds prescribed her, and appointed how far she should flee. For as soon as she leaves them, she presently scoureth along the side of some grove or
high wood, where she does ordinarily use to perch and take the stand.

The Hobbye has a blew beak, but the sear of her beak and legs is
yellowe. The crynet or little black feathers under her eyes be very black,
so as most commonly they continue and proceed from the beak to the temples
or ear burres, and in like manner, is there an other black streake that
descends to either side of her gorge. As touching the top of the head, it is
betwixt black and yellowe, but has two white seames upon the neck. The
plumes under the gorge and about the brows, are reddishe, without spot or
drop. The plumes under the belly (or as I may best term them) the breast
feathers, are broune for the most part, and yet poudered with white spots,
as Ermynes. All the back, the train and the wyngs are black aloft, she has
no great scales upon her legs, unless it be a few that begin behind the
three stretchers and pouncies, which are very large in respect of her short
legs. Her brayle feathers are engouted twixte red and black. The pendant
feathers (which are those behind the thigh) are of a rustie and smokie vernish
complexion. When a man sees her sore aloft in the air, he will judge her under
the wings, that her plumage and down, as well of her wings as betwixt her
legs, is russet and reddishe mayled.

There are two fowls, whereof the one is called ( Ian le blancke) which I take
to be the Harrohen or capped Kyte, and the other ( Blanch queue) the ring
tail, who do always flee with her for company, beating and sousing the
Larkes, and if happily they spy the Hobbye encountering the Lark, whom they
put and force so her wings, it is a pleasure to behold the game that is
betwixt this cast of bussardes and the hawke. For their desire and intent is to
bereave the sillye Hobbye her pray: but she being nimble and wight of wing,
encountreth with them, entercepting the Lark from them, manger their might and
sundry times they buckle so together, as you shall see them come tu^bling down
both yfeare, one fast griping and seized on the other.

Some would have that this Hobby of whom I write, should be that bird whom
Aristotle calls Hipotriorchis, and the Latins Subuteo: but I am not of that
mind, but that it should rather be the Sacret whom Aristotle does mean. But
let the learned reader judge the controversy, I am to lay down their natures
and properties, and not to decyde any matter of controversy which in deed
does belong to the curious Falconer, and not to him that does embrace more the
sport, than the diversity and odds of speech, which in every art a man
shall be assured to find. Let it suffice if I give the Reader to understand
the nature of every hawke now adays in use, and withal (according to my
promise and meaning) do let him know the mean to fly with them both the
fyelde and brook, as also to do him to wit, how to Mewe, Ympe, and cure
them being diseased. These are the special points, and such as deserve thanks
from me, and commendation from him. I will proceed in the description of the
nature of this hawke, according to the opinion of the Italian.

The Italians opinion of the Hobbye.
THe Hobbies are more large than the Merlyns, and for beak, eyes, plume, and
foot, they very much resemble the Falcon. They will lie upon their wings
reasonably well, following me^, and spanels, fleing upon them many turns, to the
end that when any Partridge or Quayle is sprung, they may the better stoop
from their wings, and so seize on the foul, which sundry times they do.

These kind of hawks are used of such as go with nets, and spanels. The
order of which game is this.

The dogs they range the field to spryng the foul, and the Hobbies they
accustom to flee alofte over the^, soring in the air, whom the sellie birds
espying at that advantage, and fearyng this conspiracy (as it were) betwixt
the dogs and Hawks, for their undoing and confusion, dare in no wise
commit themselves to their wings, but do lie as close and flat on the ground,
as they possible may do, and so are taken in the nets.

some Gentlemen have made report, and for truth assured me, that the
(Emperor Ferdinando) of famous memory, did give his Falconers in charge to
keep and reclayme sundry Hobbies. And his Majesty diverse times for
recreation, would take his horse, and into the fields, with a Hobbie on his
fiste, holding in his right hand a long sle^der pole, or reed seven foot in
length, on the top whereof there was conveyed by sleight, a strong line, with
a sliding knotte: And, when happily his Majesty had espied a larcke on the
ground, he would forthwith hold up, and advance his Hobbie, to the vewe of the
sellie bird, whom as soon as the larcke saw, she would in nowise dare to
spryng, but lie as still as a stone, flat upon the earth, so fearful they are
of the Hobbie in chief of al other Hawks: then would the Emperor at his good
leisure and great pleasure, with his long pole, and the sliding line, take the
sellie foul, and draw her up unto him, and truly took no small delight in
this kind of pastime, and would cause his Falconers to do likewise, who by
this devise took many birds, and in this sort would they Hawke from the
beginning of September, to the end of October.

This practice did somewhat resemble, and draw to the nature of our devise, in
daring of larckes, which we use at these days, but (in my judgment) nothing
so ready and fit as our pastime and ginne which we have, which is a very
good sport and full of delight, to see the fearful nature of the silly
Larcke, with the great awe and subjection that the Hobbie has her in, by the
law of kind: for assuredly there is no other Hawke, no not the hugest, whom
the larcke does so much fear as ye Hobbie which may manifestly appear by this
that I have written, as also by daily experience and practice in that behalf.

Of the Goshawke, after the opinion of William Tardiff a Frenchman.
depiction of goshawk

A general division of Goshawkes, whom the French men call Autour.
THere are (says he) five kinds of Autours or Goshawkes, speaking of the
Goshawke in the largest name and nature, comprysed in that word, Autour.

The first and most noble kind, is the female Goshawke, which is with us
most ordinarily in use.

The second is named a demi Autour, or Goshawke, as it were a kind betwixt two
other sorts, and that is a spare slender hawke and of little regard, in
respect of any good she will do.

The third is the Tyercle which is the male, or cock to the Goshawke, who
does slay the Partridge, and is not of sufficient force to kill the Crane. He
is termed a Tyerclet, for that there are most commonly disclosed three birds
in one self eyree, two Hawks, and one Tiercell.

The fourth kind of Autour is ye Sparowhawke, whose nature is to kill all kind
of pray that the Goshawke does, save only the larger sort of fowls.

The fifth kind is called ( Sabech) whom the Egiptians term ( Baydach) which
does very much resemble the Sparowhawke, but is less than the Sparowhawke, and
has a very blewe eye.

There are sundry sorts of Goshawkes, and those brought and conveyed out of
sundry forayne parts and regions, but among the^ all, that Goshawke that is
bred and eyreed in Armenia, and Persia, is the principal best hawke, and then
next to her in goodness, the hawke of Greece, and lastly that of Afrike.

The hawke of Armenia has her eyes green, but ye best of yt kind is she that
has black eyes, and black plumes on her back.

The hawke of Persia is large, well plumed, clear and deep eyed, with hangyng
and pendant eyleddes and brows.

The hawke of Greece has a great head, well seasoned, a strong neck, and is
reasonable well plumed.

the Goshawke of Afrike, has black eyes in her soarage, but being a mewed
hawke, her eyes become reddish and fiery.

At what times Hawks begin to fall to liking, which is at Cawking time, all
birds of pray do assemble themselves with the Goshawke, and do flock
together. As namely the Falcon, the Sacre, and such other that live on pray
and rauine.

And hereof it proceeds that the Goshawkes become so diverse in goodness,
force, and hardiness, according to the diversity of their choice, and cawkyng.

The best Goshawke ought to be weighty, and a heavy bird, as those of great
Armenia be.

In Syria they make choice of their Hawks, by the Massynes and poyse of them,
and do esteem ye most weighty Hawke for the best: as for the mayle and
conditions they do little regard or prize.

The blancke Goshawke is the largest, the fairest, and most apt and easy to
be reclaymed, and withal the strongest of all Hawks of that sort, for she
can kill the Crane. And by reason she is eyreed in a very hie and lofty place,
and ca^ best endure the cold, which is most frife in the middle region of the
air, therefore is she good to flee all fowls of that sort and condition.

The Goshawke that does incline and tend to a black maile, and that has
superfluous plumes on her head, reachyng down her front or forehead, like a
peruque, or borrowed hair, that is a very fair Hawke for beauty, but nothing
strong.

The good proportion and shape of a Goshawke.
SHe ought to have a small head, her face long and straight like the Vultur or
Eagle, a large wind pipe or throat, great eyes, deep set, and the apple or
middle part of the eye black, nares, ears, back and feet, large and
blancke, a black long beak, long neck, big breast, hard flesh, long
thighs, fleshie, and distant one from the other, the bone of the leg and
knee short, long and large pounces, and talons.

The shape from the stern or train, to the breast forward, ought to grow to a
roundenesse. The feathers of the thighs toward the train should be large,
and the train feathers, short, soft, and somewhat tending to an iron mayle.

The brayle feathers ought to be, like the breast feathers, and the covert
feathers of the train, should be spotted, and full of black rondels, but the
color of the very extremitrie and point of every train feather, ought to be
black streaked. Of mayle and color, the best is the red, somewhat tending to
black or plain grieseld.

The signs of a good Goshawke, are haughty courage, desire and greedy lust
to feed, often tyring and plucking of her meat, suddayne snatching of her
food upon the fiste, good enduing, and great force in assayling her game.

The sign of boldness in a Goshawke is this, tie her in an open light
place, and after a while darcken and obscure it, by shutting some window or
such like device, then touch her upon the suddayne at unawares, if she then
iumpe, and leppe to the fiste without fear or astonishment, that is an assured
sign of hardiness in a Goshawke.

The token of force in a Goshawke, is this, tie diverse Goshawkes in sundry
places of one self chamber or mewe, and that Hawke that does s^ise and mewte
hiest and farthest of from her, undoubtedly is the stro^gest Hawke, for that
one point declares and argues a good strong back in the Hawke.

A token of goodness and excellency in those demie Gosshawkes, whom my Author
does term ( petite Autours) is to have large and clear eyes, a small head,
long neck, low and close plume or down, hard flesh, a green seere of her
foot, large stretchers and not gowtie or fleshie, quick enduyng, large
panell, and able to slyse far from her when she mewteth. The point of the
beak to be black is a very good sign.

The ill shape of Goshawkes.
ALbeit there be a general rule, that ( contraria contrarijs dinoscuntur)
which is that one contrary is known sufficiently by the other, and therefore
having made you full show of the good shape of Goshawkes, the ill proportion
will easily thereby fall out and be descerned of it self without any farther
travail, yet nevertheless followyng mine Author, I think it not amiss to
decypher you the ill form of a Goshawke: which is, to have a great head, a
short neck, to be thick and gross plumed, soft fleshed, short thighed,
long armed, short talons, tawnie hewed, tending to black, and hard and rough
under the foot.

A Goshawke that when she is loose in the house, flees as though she were at
large and liberty, breakyng out of a mewe having great gross feathers, eyes
as red as blood, that is evermore batyng, and being set on the perch,
offreth to flee at the face of a man, such a Hawke if she be kept low in
flesh can not be borne on the fiste, if she be hie and full of flesh, she will
not then abide with her keeper, but rangle and gadde: wherefore of such Hawks
there is no account to be made at all.

A fearful Goshawke is hardly to be reclaymed and manned, for the fear she
has will always cause her to refuse the fiste and lure, and make her check,
and not willingly repair to any devise wherewith she is called and rappelde,
after her flight: which is a very great inconvenience in a Goshawke, and no
small hindrance to the sport of him that shall happen to have such a
fearful Hawke: for commonly unless they be first fond of the keeper, and
in love with the call, they will not flee their game to the likyng of their
owner, and the tediousness in coming by them again after the flight, does
breed forgetfulness of the pastime, how good and delectable soever it were
before.

That Goshawke, that has pendant plumes over her eyes, and (as they say in the
Country) whose feathers hang in her light, the white of whose eye is very
watrishe and blancke, that is read maylde, or bright tawnie, has the most
assured token that may be of ill conditions, and is not like to be well
coming. But if happily such a Hawke fall once to be good, she will then prove
a passing Hawke.

Sometimes (though very seldom) do we see a Goshawke of badde shape and in
condition clean contrary to those signs that ought to be looked for in a good
Goshauke, prove light, lusty, able to hold out and maintain her flight, and
such a one as will very well slay the greater sort of fowls.

The Goshawkes pray, is the Fezant, the Malard, the wild Goose, the Hare and
Conie: besides all which she will strike ventrouslie, and seize on a Kidde or
Goat, and keep him play so long, as the dogs at length shall come in to
assyste her and further the fall of it, which does manifestly decypher the
great inestimable courage, and valor of the Hawke.

Out of the French I have collected this concerning the Goshawke.
SOme men have thought, and been of this resolute mind, that the (Autour) or
Goshawke has been of the kind of a Vultur, for the affinity and nearenesse of
their terms and names: for (Autour) in the French, is that Hawke whom we call
the Goshawke, and (Vantour) is the Vultur, which two terms, as you see, draw
very near to one speech.

Some other have been of opinion that betwixt ye Goshawke and Sparowhawke, is
no odds or difference in nature, save only in respect of the hugeness of that
one, and the slenderness of the other: but my purpose is to treat of the
Goshawke severally from the Sparowhawke, and so to proceed to the Sparowhawke,
of whom I will write, according to the French and Italian Authors, in a
several chapter by it self, to avoid the confusion, which otherwise might
happen in that behalf.

The Goshawke is ever more regarded than her Tiercell, for the males, or cocks
among Hawks and birds of pray, do make evident proof and show to the eye,
of their difference from the femals and Hawks.

Again we may with ease discern the Goshawke from her Tyercell, for that she
is far larger, tha^ the Tiercell of her kind.

The Falconers and Ostregers, have to these two sorts, added a third kind (as
I said before) whom they term the Demygoshawke, as a bird indifferent,
betwixt the other two.

Both kinds of them are more hie, and longer armed than either the Falcon, or
Gerfalcon: they are Hawks of the fiste, and (as we call them) round winged
Hawks, quite contrary to those I wrote of before, all which are Hawks of
the lure, and long winged Hawks, otherwise called Tower Hawks.

The Hawke (I mean the female) is uery much like the Egle in mayle, and if we
may make bold to compare the less with ye larger, she has a more stately
high neck than the Egle, and of a more read or iron mayle, the grownde of her
plume and down tending to a red color.

Those Goshawkes that are of Slauonia, are good at all manner of game, large,
hardy, and fair plumed, their toungs black, and their nares great and wide.

There are Goshawkes, whom the Italians call, Alpisani, or hawks of the Alps,
which are much used in Lombardie, and Tuscane, they are more thick than they
are long, fierce and hardy.

But those Goshawkes that our Ostregers have now adays, are chiefly conveyed
out of Almayne, having their eyes and the fear of the beak as also of their
feet and legs, yellow, contrary to the Gerfalcon, whose fear is blew and
azure.

Their traynes are garnished with large drops or spots, crossing the
feather, party black, and party gray, as also the plumes of the neck and
head, are more toward a russet, and powdered with black, but those of the
thighe and under the belly or pannell, are otherwise marked, for they are not
full so yellowe, having round drops on them, not much unlike those that are
on the Peacockes train.

The Goshawkes of Almaine are not very fair though they be large Hawks,
read mayled, and yet not hardy.

There are sundry of them good in their soarage, but being once mewed prove
nothing worth: there be diverse of the^ taken in the forest of Arde, and in
sundry places of Almaine. The Greeks have called the Goshawke Hierax, the
Latins Accipiter stellaris, and the Italians, Astuy.

Thus much have I collected out of another French Author, as necessarily
belonging to the description of the nature of a Goshawke, because you shall see
the several opinions of sundry writers, and gather to your own use, what
shall occur and thwarte best with your liking, for it is not the mayle and
plume of the Hawke that I so greatly regard, or do mean to stand upon, as the
making, reclayming, diseases, and cures of the said Hawks, each one after
their proper nature and quality, if so my health will give me leave to run
mine authors through advisedly, according to my meaning and resolution, at what
time I first undertook this collection.

Out of the Italian concerning the Goshawkes and their kinds.
THere are sundie sorts of Goshawkes, according to the diversity of places
and regions. There be Hawks of Armenia, Slauonia, Sardinia, Calament, of the
Alps, which they use in Lombardie, Tuscan, Marca, and Puglia, some other of
Russia, Friuli, Almania, and othersome of Lombardie, all which I will briefly
touch unto you, and not long dwell in the matter, having out of my french
authors already deciphered the natures of the most part of them.

First of al there are Goshawkes, called Armenia Hawks, much differing from
the Goshawke, in sort as almost, they have no resemblance at all to the other
kinds of Goshawkes. They are very fair and huge, the mayle of them is blancke,
as sundry haggart Falcons be, they flee with great courage and life, all
greater sort of fowls.

There are others eyried in Slauonia and Dalmacia, and thence are they termed
Slauon Goshawkes, which indeed are good for any thing a man will employ them
unto. Very fair and hardy Hawks, large footed, very well penned, their down
and plumage excellent fine, their tungs black, and their nares large and wide.

Those of Sardinia are nothing like the other Hawks, they are brown, and
russet plumed, final Hawks, hard and not small footed, and nothing ventrous.

Those of Calament are short trussed Hawks, and large, blancke seared on the
foot, those flee the greater fowls exceeding well.

The Goshawkes of the Alps, and of Calabria, are in a manner more large than
they are long, very proud, and hardy Hawks.

The Goshawkes of Lombardie are not very large, brown mayld, and cowardly
kytes to do any good.

The Goshawke of Russia and Sarmatia, is a large and huge Hawke, the most part
of them are blancke Hawks, and taken up of great Princes and noble states,
they are apt and able to do any thing that may be looked for from Hawks of
that kind. My self have seen great store of them in the City of Mosqua,
which is the chief dukedom of all Russia. The Moscouits and Tartarians do
use to flee with those Goshawkes at the brook, and there do beat up the foul
with y^e drum, without which you shall seldom see a Boyaron (as they term
them) which is a Gentleman, ride at any time. And one special thing which I
noted among them was, that as well Moscouites, as Tartares, do use to bear
their Hawks on the right fiste, which is clean contrary to our manner and
guise here in England, or in any other Region that I have heard or seen, save
only in those North parts, no reason I can yield for it, but that, each
country for the most part has his fashion.

Those of Friuli, are good Hawks, and large, but not so fair as the Slauon
Hawks.

Note this, that a good Goshawke ought to be littell, and broad shouldred,
large brested, very round and fleshly, having a long thigh, a short leg or
arm, and the same great, and a large foot, and not gowty but slender.
Contrariwise the Tiercell should be large, for it is a common saying.

A little Hawke, and a large Tiercell, is ever best.

All Goshawkes are by nature greedye, and catching of whom some we use to flee
the river, and fresh brooks, and some the Sea, and othersome again the field,
and never or very seldom the river or brook.

The first sort for the most part do pray upon Duckes, Geese, Hearons,
Shouelers, and such like foul, as do usually haunt, and live in the Sea and
rivers. And those diverse times do seize and take their pray upon the suddayne
at unawares, by fleeing low near the ground, and stealing upon the foul.

The other, after a while that they have used to flee the field, do pray on
Pigeons, Pullets, Hens, and Partridges. And being once mewed hawks, and past
their soarage, they will take the stand upon some tree, and finding either
Patridge, Fezant, Pullet, Hen, or such other like foul, they make their
stowping so fiercely and in such great have, and do flee them so far before
head and at randon, mayntayning, and making good their flight, as in the end
they kill them, and do pray upon them.

Of the Goshawkes, those that be ventrous, and hardy, will kill the Hare, and
having kilde him, diverse times, they swallow in for have great bones, and do
put them over very well, and endue them safely without any hurt at all.

Those that are the river Goshawkes, and do haunte the water, and brooks, are
commonly the most hardy and ventrous Hawks of all that kind, and do at the
river of their own inclination and nature fall to kill the great fowls of
the River, of which I have before made recital and mention.

Truly the Goshawke is very much to be regarded for her hardy metal and
courage, for that therein she is not inferior to any kind of Hawke, but
rather more fearce and eger. And again to be kept with greater care, for that
she is more choice and daynty, and does look to have a more nice hard kept
on her, than any other kind of Falcon or Hawke, unless it be y^e Sparowhawke,
which is all one in a manner in nature with the Goshawke, and of whom I purpose
now to write.

Of the Sparovvhawke out of the french Authors.
depiction of sparrowhawk

I Will now write somewhat of the Sparhawke, for that she is in her kind, and
for that game that her strength will give her leave to kill, a very good Hawke,
and much used in France, And besides, he that knows well how to man,
reclayme, and flee with the Sparhawke, may easily know how to keep, and deal
with all other Hawks. Moreover it is a Hawke, that serves both winter and
somer, with great pleasure, and the game that she flees is ordinary, and
common to be had, and she will generally flee at all kind of game, more than
ye Falcon, or than any other kind of Hawke will. And the winter Sparhawke, if
she prove good, will kill the Pie, the Iaye, the chough, the Woodcocke, the
Thrush, the black Bird, the Veylfare, and sundry other sorts of birds.

The diversity of Sparhawkes according to their times and ages.
1 THe Eyasse hawke, is she that is taken in the eyree.

2 The brancher, is she that follows the old Hawke, from branch to branch,
and tree to tree, which is also termed a ramage Hawke.

3 The Sore Hawke, is she that has flien, and prayed for her, and is taken
before she mewe.

4 The fourth kind, is that Hawke, that is mewed, and has cast her soar
feathers.

The good shape and proportion of a Sparhawke.
SParhawkes are of diverse ploumes, some are small plumed, and blanke Hawks,
othersome of a larger feather, which are not so good in our opinion as for
their shape. The Hawke that is well shapt is large and short, with a slender
head, large, and broad shouldred, big armed, large and wide footed, and
black maylde, with a good great beak, her eyes somewhat hollow, and deep
set, blanke eye liddes, the sear of her beak twixt green and white, a hie
big neck, long wings, reaching quite athwart the body of the Hawke, so as
the point of the wing meet with the top of the train very near, and that
her train be not ouerlong, but of a reasonable broad feather, sharp pownces,
small and black, and evermore well disposed to feed hungerly and with great
appetite.

The Nyasse Hawke is good, and will come to the fist very well, and not lightly
soar away, or be lost.

The Sore Hawke is hard to be manned, but will prone good, if she will once
brook company, this Hawke, for that she has prayed for her self, is very
ventrous and hardy.

The best Sparowhawke, is that Hawke whom we call the brauncher.

What kinds of Sparowhawkes there are.
THere are Sparowhawkes, whom the Italians call ( diventimiglia) which are
large and long Hawks, with a great beak, large foot, and with thirteen
feathers in the train. Those Hawks are excellent to flee any kind of game.

There are other called Slauon Hawks, good for all purposes, and full of
hardiness, long and large Hawks, having a great long beak, and black breast
feathers.

There are others of Calabria not very large, but of great courage, plumed
like the Quayle, that will do according as they are taught and manned.

There be sparowhawkes eyreed in Corsica and brought from Sardinia, small
Hawks, brown or canuas maylde, that will flee very well.

Those of Almania are very slender, and nothing good.

The Hawks of Verona and Vicentia, are of mean size, and many of them do
prove to be good Hawks.

There be Sparohawkes called Alpisani, of the Alps, that are large, wight of
wing, and ventrous to flee any kind of foul.

There are others eyreed in ye bale of Sabbia, of a reasonable lise, russet
maylde, entermedled with golden spots, or drops, like the turtle, those be
very good to flee great fowls.

There is one other kind of Sparowhawkes, eyreed in Bergamasca, in a vallie
called the Black vale, near the confines of Voltolina, slender Hawks, brown
mayld, good to be manned and reclaymed, and those are the principallest of all
other Sparowhawkes.

I do not here in this place deal exactly of the mayles, and plumes of these
kind of hawks, in part for that the hawks are of sundry and several
plumes, according to the diversity of countries and regions, where they are
eyreed: and part, for that the hawks themselves are so ordinary in use, as it
were to be esteemed but a superfluous labor, to waste much time therein, in
penning of that, which is (in the opinion of men) of no great importance. My
chief care and industry (if health allow me leave, and sickness too much
offend not my ease) shall consist in the reclayming and ma^ning of all these
kinds of hawks, according to their natures and properties, and in displaying
the means to flee with them, and to keep them, both for the field and
brook. And after that, in declaration of their diseases, ordinarily incidente
unto their kinds, and the best remedies for the same, which (I doubt not)
are the only and chiefest points that the discrete and learned reader will
accept from me, and such as will most stand him in stead that does mean
to deal with hawks. Wherefore I thus make the Epiloge and conclusion of the
first part of my treatise and collection, wherein are contained all the kinds,
names, and the causes of those names, of all such Hawks and birds of pray as
are most in use, and regarded among noble men and gentlemen at these days,
craving the Reader to bestow no less good liking upon the translation and
collection hereof (if it in any part deserve it) than I have employed
travail and pains in the true search and examination of the same, both out of
the French and Italian Authors, where I must confess, I have not translated
Verbatim, and by word of line what I found (for then had I not dealt so
exactly as I now have done, for that I found sundry things not so well
agreeing to our humors and use,) but have taken my pleasure of them, in making
choice of the chiefest matter, which did occur in them, hoping, that the
more my pains have bin the less shall be mine offense, and the greater the
liking of the Reader, and the better his acceptaunce: which if I find, both I
for my travail, shall think my self sufficiently guerdoned, and the careful
Printer deem both his cost and charge well employed, being meante, to the
benefyte and pleasure of his native countrymen, whose avail he chiefly
respects herein, and not any great advantage that shall privately fall out
to him.

Of the Matagasse.
THough the Matagasse be a Hawke of none account, or price, neither with us in
any use, yet nevertheless, for that in my division I made recital of her
name, according to the French Author, from whence, I collected sundry of those
points and documents, appertayning to Falconrie: I think it not beside my
purpose, briefly to describe here unto you, though I must needs confess,
that where the Hawke is to so slender value, the definition, or rather
description of her nature and name must be thought of no great regard.

The shape of her is this.

She is beaked and headded like the Falcon, her plume is of two colors, her
breast white, her eye, beak, and foot black, a long black train, her
flagges and long feathers partly black, and white, and the color of those
feathers she changes not, though she mewe never so often.

Her feeding is upon Rattes, Squirrells, and Lisards, and sometimes upon
certain birds she does use to pray, whom she does intrappe and deceive by
flight, for this is her devise.

She will stand at pearche upon some tree or post, and there make an exceeding
lamentable cry, and exclamation, such as birds are wont to do, being
wronged, or in hazard of mischief, and all to make other fowls believe and
think that she is very much distressed, and stands needful of aid,
whereupon the credulous sellie birds do flock together presently at her call
and voice, at what time if any happen to approach near her, she out of hand
seazeth on them, and deuoureth them, (ungrateful subtle foul) in requital
of their simplicity and pains.

These hawks are in no account with us, but poor simple fellows and
peasantes sometimes do make them to the fiste, and being reclaymed after their
unskillful manner, do bear them hooded, as Falconers do their other kinds of
hawks whom they make to greater purposes.

Here I end of this foul, because I neither account her worth the name of
a hawke, in whom there rests no valor or hardiness, ne yet deserving to
have any more written of her property and nature, more than that she was in
mine Author specified, as a member of my division, and there reputed in the
number of long winged hawks. For truly it is not the property of any other
hawke, by such devise and cowardly wile to come by their pray, but they love
to win it by main force of wings at randon, as the round wynged hawks do,
or by free stowping as the hawks of the Tower do most commonly use, as the
Falcon, Gerfalcon, Sacre, Merlyn, and such like, which do lie upon their
wing, roding in the air, and ruff the foul, or kill it at the encounter.

I cannot say, that at any time I have seen this kind of hawke, neither in
any book read of her nature and disposition, as I have here made mention of
it, save only in my authur, who writing of Falconrie, was so bold as to
rank her among other hawks, of greater account and valewe, and in Gesner,
where he treats of all kind of birds and fowls, where, I remember well, I
have read of the name and nature of the Matagasse, and there have seen her
proportion and shape set down in colors, such as I have before declared
you in this chapter. and, in my judgment, no odds or difference to be
found, betwixt Gesnerus description, and mine authors, in that behalf. ()

The Second part or book, of this collection of Falconrie.
Certain special points, necessary for a Falconer, or Ostreger, collected
out of the Italian Author.
depiction of man with falcon

Having made you sufficient show in the former part of this book, and
collectio^, of all kinds of hawks serving to Falconrie, it shall not be amiss
to deliver you some special and necessary rules, due to a good Falconer.

First, it is behooueful for a Falconer to be very diligent and inquisitiue
to learn and mark the qualitye and mettell of his hawks, and to know which
hawke he shall flee withal early, and with which late, because all hawks are
not disposed or mettelde alike. Wherefore, the first and special observation
is, to note the natural inclination and disposition of his hawks in that
behalf.

Then next is it necessary for him to be fond over his hawke, patte^t, and
withal careful to keep her clean out of lise, mytes, and all such other
diseases, as I shall hereafter treat of in the latter part of this
collection, with such remedies, as I shall lay down for every grief. And of
the two, he must rather keep his hawke hye, and full of flesh, than poor
and low. Besides that, this is one general rule, which by experience you
shall find to be most true, that all kinds of hawks are more subject to
infirmities, being poor and low, than when they are lusty and full in flesh.

Every night, after he has fleen with his hawke the day, either at the
field, or brook, he must give his hawke casting, somewhyles plumage, some
other while pellets of Cotton, or such like, and again, sometimes some one
medicine or other, according as by her casting, or mewte, he shall perceive her
to stand needful thereof, which point I will more at large describe, in
another place proper and peculiar to that matter.

Every night, he must not forgette to make the place very clean under the
pearche, so as he may both find the casting of his hawke, and be certainly
assured whither she has already cast or not, whereby he may the better judge
and discern her state. For by the casting is found, whither the hawke do
need either upward, or downward scowrings, or stones, or any such like
remedy.

He must remember every evening to tie out his hawke a weathering, save only
in such days, as she has bathed before, for because then the taking
ouermuche moisture, will breed her a thousand evils and inconveniences. For
such euenings as she has bathed the day, she ought of right to be placed in
some warm chamber on a pearche, with a candle burning by her, where she must
sit vnhooded, if so she be gentle, and not rammage, to the end she may trick
her self, and rejoice by enoyling her after the water, before she flee again.

Every morning early he must not forgette to set her out to wether her, where
if she have not already cast, she may cast, and there keep her hooded,
till such time as she go to the field.

In feeding his hawke, he must beware of giving her two sorts of meat at
one time, to gorge her withal, neither must he give her such flesh, as has
any evil savor, and is not sweet, but must respect to allow her wholesome
meats, for breeding ill diseases. For Hawks are dayntie birds, in their
kind, and the more to be considered of, when they are in hand, under a
Falconers keeping and usage, because they were wont to pray for themselves at
liberty, and therein follow such law and order, as nature had prescribed
them, but being restrained, the course of kind is quite altered in them, and
therefore the greater art and regard to be used for them. Art must supply the
restraintes of kind, by cunning.

He must beware, if happily he have occasion of necessary business, at his
departure from home, not to leave his hawke tied on a pearche of any great
heyght from the ground, for fear of bating and hanging by the heels, for
then either will she cast her gorge, or otherwise spoil her self: but she
must be placed on a low block, or stone, and if there be mo hawks than one,
they must be sundred so far one from the other, as they may not approach or
reach one the other, neither with beak, talons, or otherwise, because their
nature is to byte, and buckle together, if they come within reach.

When he addresseth him to make his flighte with his Falcon, it is behoouefull
for him to have all her fellow Falconers, or such as have hawks in the field,
to set down their hawks on the ground, to be in the more readiness to
assiste him in his purpose, and to tie them sure, for fear of ill accidents
that may befall them.

And again, at the river, he must skillful to land his foul so placing the
residue of his company, and their hawks, as they may flee eke without any
encounter, which is not only the loss of the foul, and hinderance to their
sport, but also the ruin and spoil of their hawks on both parts. He must
be careful that his hawke keep her gate, and flee it good, so as in no wise
he pluck her not down, nor make her bate of her pitche.

He must always be assured to have mummy in powder in his bag in a
readiness, whatsoever should happen, with such other medicines as I shall
hereafter treat of, for that it may so fall out, as his hawke may receive a
broose at the encounter of a foul. Moreover he must not be unfurnished of
Aloes washed, Cloves, Nutmugges, Saffron, casting, cryance, and such like
necessary implementes. And he must remember that his Aloes be shining and
clear, for then is it of the best sort of Aloes.

Lastely he must be able to make his lures, hooddes, of all sorts, Iesses,
bucts, and other needful furniture for his hawke, and must not be without
store thereof to allow his betters and states in the field, if happily they
want any such devises. He cannot well be without his coping yrons, to cope his
hawks beak if it be ouergrowen, which will be a hinderance to her feeding,
and to cope her pownces and talons, if need be. He must have his cauterizing
buttons, and other iron or silver tools, to cauterize or burn his hawks if
cause require such cure. For having all these necessaries, and doing as I have
and will tell you, all his game shall succeed and sort well, and he be assured
for ye most part of good pastime in ye field, when other ignorant gromes shall
both lack sport, and loose their hawks, the greatest corsie yt may happen to
a gentleman yt loves ye game.

Let these few aduertisementes and instructions suffice in this place, if
other wynts necessary not recited here, be remembered at the full in any other
part of this book, I crave but thanks for my pains, and courtesy at the
Falconers hands, for whose learning and pleasure I partly and chiefly wrote
this collection.

The first instruction is, how to make a Falcon and other hawks fleeing, after
the opinion of Ian de Franchiers.
FIrst, let your hawke be taken on the fiste, and hoodded, then let her be
watched three days and nights, before you vnhoode her, and feed her always
hooded in an easy rufter hoode. At the end of three days you may vnhoode
her, and feed her vnhooded, and when she is fed, hoode her again, so that
she be not vnhooded (but when you feed her) until she know her meat: then
when she begins to be acquainted with you, hoode her and vnhood her
oftentimes, to the end she may the better abide the hoode. But use her gently,
and be pacient with her at the first, and to the end your hawke may be the
better manned, and the sooner reclaimed, you shall do well to bear her commonly
in places where most people do frequent, and where most exercises are used. And
when she is well manned, make her come a little to the fiste for her meat. And
when you have showed her the perch or stock, and tied her upon it, put with
her upon the said pearche or stock some Pullet, or other quick foul as
often as you may, and let her feed thereon at pleasure until she be
reasonably gorged, and do in like manner upon the lewer until she know it
perfectly. Afterward you may give her more liberty, and lewer her with a
cryance, lewring her twice a day further and further of. And when she is
thoroughly lewred, you shall teach her to flee upon you, until she know both
how to get to her gate, and to flee rownde upon you. Then shall you cast her
out some quick foul, and when she has stowped and seized upon it, you
shall suffer her to plume it and to foot it at her pleasure, giving her a
reasonable gorge thereon, as is before said, and continewing always to reward
her upon the said lewer, in such sort, that she never find the lewer without
some reward tied upon it, and by that means she will always love the lewer
and her keeper well, and will not lightly rangle or be lost. Thus you may
continue her forty days, or thereaboutes, and then you may flee with her safe
enough. But before you so do, let her be skowred and bathed, and fed with
clean meat, and well washed, gyuing her casting every night, even as men use
to give fleing hawks.

The manner of fleyng with hawks, as well to the field as to the River, and
first to the River, according as Martine teaches.
YOu must understand that the river hawke ought to be let into the wind, and
above her pray to get the vauntage of her gate, and to be at her pitche:
then shall you make in toward the pray. And when they are got up to their
full pitche, run upon the foul, and land them, laying them out from of the
water, and if you fail in doing thereof, then you should take down your hawke
with some pullete, Pigeon. or other quick foul, to teach, and the better to
win such hawks as are but lately entered, until they know their pray and
their fleing perfectly. ()

To fly at the Hearon according to Martine.
depiction of men, one on horseback, with dogs and birds

THere is another manner of fleing which is called the flight at the Hearon,
this is the noblest flight of all others. For the Hawke ought to be well
lewred and well trained to get up to an high gate, and therewithal she must
well know a quick foul, and such a Falcon as is apt to flee, the Hearon
should not be flowen with, to any other kind of foul but only to the Hearon
most commonly. For as much as amongst all other flights, there is no such
mountey made, nor such force used as in the flight at the Hearon, and
therefore reason would that such Falcons should not be flowen withal or
inured to any meaner or less pray than the Hearon. For if a Hawke be a good
Hearoner, it is sufficient, and if after your Hawke have flowen the Hearon,
you should let her flee any other sleighter foul or pray, She will lightly (by
your own default) become a slugge and take disdain, in such sort that (where
before she was a good Hearoner) she will be so no more, and will turn to her
own ease, so that she will never care to flee the Hearon. For as much as she
will give her self to pray upon foul that is more easy to reach, and will
forget or forslow her valiant hardiness, the which is much to be lamented if
a man have once a good Hearoner, and do so spill her. Nevertheless you may
flee with the Sacre all manner of foul more easily than with any other kind of
Hawke, because she is alike common to all foul, even so is she hard to be
made, and of a hard and dull capacity, but despair not therefore, for in the
end they prove good if the Falconer take such pains with them as he ought to
do.

For the flight to the field as Master Ame Cassian teaches.
SOme kind of Hawks there be which are made for the field. For as much as
there be some men which delight more to have Hawks for the field than for the
river, the Hawks which are good to flee the field, are first entered by the
Spaniels, and as well accustomed to know them by the hear and proportion, as
they know their pray by the feather and flight, and secondarily they are also
entered by customary knowledge of their pray, and therefore it is not lightly
possible that this kind of pastime should be perfectly handled, unless the
dogs and Hawks be so well aquainted and known one to the other, that each
of them do love others, for although naturally the Hawke be hardly entered to
become familiar with the dogs, and will not lightly nor at the first love
them, yet maruayle you not thereat, for in the end all field Hawks will love
them, and become familiar with them, but to bring that to pass, you must
continually bear your Hawke amongst Spanyels, and aquaint her with them, that
she may the better abide them, and that being done, the oftener that your
Hawke flee the field, the truer you shall find mine opinion: and surely you
may easily have good Hawks to the field, if you keep them in good order and
diet as reason requires, giving them upon their first, second, and third pray,
a reasonable good gorge, and afterward you may withdraw and abate your reward
by little and little, to make your Hawke the easlyer forget it, for by that
means you shall make your Hawke know her live foul the better, if you
reward her with the head and brains of the foul taken, and so of every one
which she shall slay, until you would give her a gorge, at time and hour
convenient, and by that means, you may have a good field Hawke unless the
fault be your own.

Other flights to the field called great flights.
THere is yet another kind of flight to the field which is called the great
flight, as to the Cranes, wild Geese, Bustarde, Bird of Paradise, Bittors,
Shouelers, Hearons, and many other such like, and these you may flee from the
fiste, which is properly termed the Source. Nevertheless in this kind of
Hawking which is called the great flighte, the Falcons or other Hawks cannot
well accomplish their flight, at the Crane, Bustard or such like, unless they
have the help of some Spanyell or such dog, well inured and taught for that
purpose with your Hawke. For as much as great flights require pleasant aid and
assistance, yea and that with great diligence.

Advertisement given by Master Martine to make a Hawke bold and hardy, and to
love her pray.
IF you would have your Hawke hardy, keep her oftentimes all day long upon
your fiste, and feed her with Pullets flesh early in the morning as much as
shall be sufficient for a beaching, that done set her abroad in the Sun,
with water before her, to the end she may bathe when she will, and bowze, as
naturally they are inclined to do, and it does them singular great pleasure,
for bowzing may oftentimes preserve them from sickness, and yet sometimes a
Hawke bowzeth after some disease whereof she has long languished, and dies,
or else she is thereby recured: for after such a disease, bowzing does either
cure her or quite dispatch her. Now when you have done as is before said
(whether she bathe or not) you shall take your Hawke upon your fiste, and so
keep her on the fiste until you go to bed, and when you go to bed set a
candle before her, which may last all the night, and in the morning (if she
did bath) you shall set her in the Sun for one hour until she be well
wetherd, and then afterward (if she did not bathe) take wine and water, and
therewith bespout her well with your mouth three hours after, setting her in
the Sun again, and (for lack of Sunneshyne) before the fire, until she be
very well dried, and if you be well assured that she is thoroughly enseamed
clean, and has bin well manned by the space of thirty or forty days, then
may you flee the field with her, and if you perceive that she have good
desire to flee, let her flee, and if she kill any thing, give her a good gorge
thereupon, but if she kill nothing, then feed her with the leg or the wing
of an Hen or a Pullet, washed in clean running water, keeping her still
upon the fiste as is before said: and the next day flee with her again, and
if she kill any thing, give her her reward, and keep her in this order until
she be perfectly entered and quarred: but then you must have discretion, for
sometimes by this order you may bring her low, in such sort that she should
not easily be recovered to make her flight strongly. Yet Martine says the
contrary, but if an Hawke be very hard and stubborn to her keeper in her
flight, then let her be well spowted again with luke warm water, and so set
abroad all night in the open air. In the morning let her be set either in the
Sun or before the fire, where when she has well proyned her you may go
flee with her, and if she kill and flee well, then keep her in this order and
tune, for else she may take sundry evil toys. And this precepte serves as
well for them that desire to have good Hawks for the field as otherwise: and
if you would have your Hawks love their pray, take Cynamon and Sugercandie,
of each a like quantity, and make thereof a powder, and when your Hawke has
killed any thing, and that you come to reward her, sprinkle some of that
powder upon the part wherewith you reward her, and it shall make her love
that kind of pray the better ever afterward.

How a man should use an Eyasse Hawke.
IF you have Eyasse Hawks, you shall feed them most with Pultrie, Beef, or
Gotes flesh: and this is done to keep them from ill toys: and when they be
well lewred and trained, then bear them upon the fiste hooded, and ordered in
all points according to the rule prescribed before in the first Chapter, and
after xxx. or xl. days past, bring them to the flighte, and the first,
second, and third flight, you may be fond over them, abating your favor
afterward by little and little, until they be brought in perfect tune,
spowting them oftentimes with wine and water. For (as Martine says) some
Eyasse Hawks will not much bathe them. Nevertheless you ought therein also
to use discretion, for by often bathing or spowting, you may bring your Hawke
very low, in such sort that she should have more need of a good gorge,
than of bathing, or spowting, and especially such Hawks as are fierce of
nature, and will not often bathe of themselves.

A consideration of the diversity of Hawks natures, according to Martine.
THere are some sorts of Falcons which have this diversity of nature, that
some of the^ well fly well being high and full of flesh, and some other flee
best when they are kept low. Wherefore a Falconer should have especial
consideration thereunto, for Falcons are fit for all flightes, as is before
said, but the blanke Falcons are of one nature, and the blewe Falcons of
another, and the Falcon of the reddish plume has also her properties diverse
from the rest. Nevertheless to speak as I have found, of all other Hawks
the blancke Falcon is best. And both by reason and experience I find that
she would be kept higher and in better plight than other Hawks, for you
shall see the blancke Falcon keeping a like hand upon her and other Falcons
prove higher and in better plight when she is fleeyng than any other Hawke.
And the reason is, because she is very gentle, and with more ease manned than
any other kind of Falcon, and loves her keeper better, so that thereby she
keeps hirselfe higher and in better plighte than such Hawks, as bate much
and are froward of condition. ()

How to seele a Sparowhauke and to make her fleing according to Guillam Tardif.
depiction of sparrowhawk

A Sparowhawke newly taken should be thus used, take a needle thredded with
untwisted threde, and (casting your Hawke) take her by the beak and put the
needle through her eye lidde, not right against the sight of the eye, but
somewhat nearer to the beak, because she may see backewardes. And you must
take good heed, that you hurt not the web, which is under the eyelydde, or
on the inside thereof. Then put your needle also through that other eyelydde,
drawing the ends of the thredde together, tie them over the beak, not with a
straight knotte, but cut off the thredes ends near to the knotte, and twiste
them together in such sort, that the eyeliddes may be raised so vpwardes,
that the hawke may not see at all, and when the threade shall wax loose or
untied, then the hawke may see somewhat backwardes, which is the cause that
the thredde is put nearer to the beak. For a Sparowhawke should see somewhat
backewards, and a Falcon forwards. The reason is, that if the Sparowhawke should
see forewardes, she would beat of her feathers, or break them when she bateth
upon the fiste, and seeing the company of men, or such like, she would bate to
much.

But to trim your Sparowhawke in her rightes, she must have Iesses of
lether, the which must have knottes at the end, and they should be half a
foot long, or thereabout, at the least a shaftmete between the hose of the
Iesse, and the knotte at the end, whereby you tie the hawke.

She should also have two good belles, whereby she may be the better heard.
For commonly when a Sparowhawke takes any pray, she will carry it into some
thick bushe to feed thereon, in such sort, that she cannot lightly be
either heard or seen, and whiles she plumeth it, the plumage does oftentimes
cover both her eyes, or one of them, then to take away the said plumage, she
straineth with one of her feet, and thereby her belles discover her. Therefore
if she had but one bell, she might happen to scratte with that foot which
lacks the bell, and so should not be heard. The Sparowhawkes which are
wonted to be hooded, and which will gently brook it, are much better than
they which will not be hooded. For they bate less, and are with more ease
borne in the rain, or any evil weather. For being hooded, the Falconer may
hyde and cover them with his cloak, which he cannot do to the other.
Furthermore they shall be able to flee better and more strongly, that are good
weather hawks, for they shall be less bruised, than a hawke which is not
hooded, which will weary her self with bating, and withal a man may the
better flee with the^ at advantage, because they bate not, but only when you
would have them to flee, whereby they have the greater courage, and also a man
may bear them in all places, without bating or beating themselves out of
breath.

How a man should manne a Sparowhavvke, and make her fleing.
FOr as much as Sparowhawkes are of sundry sorts of plumes, and sundry
shapes and proportions, there are also sundry manners of manning them and
making them, and there is much less pains to be taken with some one, than
with some other. For the more eager and sharper set that a Sparowhawke is,
the sooner shall you win her, and man her.

First, to win her to feed, rub her feet with warm flesh, chirping and
whistling to her, and sometimes present the flesh unto her beak, and if she
will not yet feed, rub her feet with a quick bird, and the bird will
cry, and if the Sparowhawke do seize it with her feet, it is a token that
she will feed. Then tear off the skin and feathers of the birds breaste,
and offer it to the hawks beak, and she will taste thereof. For a
Sparowhawke which feeds immediately after she be taken, does show that she
is eager, and has good appetite. And you may give her as much more at
evening, yea and sometimes in the day time, so that she be not gorged first,
but that she have put over her meat. When she is thus well entered, and will
feed when you chirpe or whistle to her, then may you hoode her with an hoode
that is large and deep enough, so that it neither hurt nor touch her eyes,
When she will endure to be hooded and vnhooded, without bating, and that she
will feed hooded, then must you abate her meals, giving her less meat, and
feed her betimes in the morning, when she has endewed, (that is to say, when
she shall have put over her meat, so as there remains nothing in her gorge)
then may you give her a beaching in the day time, taking off, and putting on
again her hoode, to make her the more eager. For it will not be amiss, to
give her a bitte or two of meat every time that you hoode her. When it is
evening, then suppe her up, giving her the head or brains of a Hen or
Pullette, until the morning. And if you perceive that she is becomme very
eager, then loose the threade wherewith she is seeled, but let it be night
first, and that she have seen backewardes, as before said. If she may
abide company, yet watch her all that night that you vnseele her, to the
end she may also be accustomed to hear people speak, and be acquaynted
with them, and when you hoode her again, give her two or three bittes of
meat. In the morning betimes, put a bird in her foot, whom if she seize
hardily, and plume thereon eagerly, then may you boldly take off her hoode,
but if she bate then, hoode her again, and watch her until she be
thoroughly won and manned: but if she feed well before company, and become
familiar and quyete before them, watch her no loger, but keep her on the
fiste some part of the night amongst company, making her to plume, giving
her now and then a bytte or two of flesh, and putting her hoode on and off
therewithal. When you go to bed, set your hawke near to your beds
headde, upon some trestle or stool, that you may wake her often in the night.
Then rise before day, and take her upon your fiste, and off with her hoode,
that she may see the people about her, and when she beholds them, put a
quick bird in her foot, as before said. When she feeds thereupon, hoode her
again, giving her the rest of your bird hooded, and when it is further forth
day, you may look whether she have any thing in her gorge, or not. If she
have nothing above, give her some little beaching, and beache her oftentimes
before company, hooding and vnhooding her. But at night she should be always
vnhooded, that she may see people, and become acquainted with them, giving her
to feed of a Hen or Pullet. To heal ye places where she was seeled, to the
end she may see the better, when you go to bed, hold her in a dark
corner, and spirte a little water upon her head, that she may frote her eyes
against the pynions of her wings. Then in the morning when she perceyueth the
day light, and has warm meat ready upon your fiste, and is clean loosed
that she may see both before and behind her, and seems to be familiar and
bold amongst people, then may you make her as before said. But remember that
you give her no plumage that day, in which you have given her washed meat,
yea and allow her no plumage until she be well manned. For until she be
thoroughly manned, she will not dare to cast. Then if you would thoroughly man
your Sparowhawke, and keep her eagre, take her early in the morning upon your
fiste, and go into some place where no body shall interrupt you, where first
cause her to plume with her beak upon some quick bird, then vnseaze her, and
set her upon some thing, and reach her your hand, and show her your siste,
giving her a bitte or two thereof. And if she come thereunto willingly, then
call her again morning and evening, further and further of, but ever before
company, to acquaynt her the better with them, fastening a long line or cryance
unto her limbs: if it be fair weather, and that the Sun shine, you should
then proffer her the water, to the end she may bathe her. Provided always
in so doing that she be sound, well manned, and that she be not poor, nor
gorged. For bathing is a thing which makes a hawke familiar and lusty, But
remember yt always after she have bathed, you give her some live bird to
feed on, and always when you call her or feed her, you must chirpe with
your mouth, or whistle, to the end she may becomme acquainted with your
whistle, and come thereunto. You must feed her amongst horses and dogs, to
make her also the better acquainted with them. If she have flowen, and you
would set her in the Sun to weather, set her upon the ground on some
coodgell or truncheon, making her fast, and she will always love the better
to sit upon the ground. After she has bathed, if you perceive your
Sparowhawke lusty, you may flee with her the next day toward the evening,
but first you must have reclaymed her to come out of a tree, and called her
to you sitting on horse back, being always provided of some Pigeon, or other
quick thing, to take her down the more easily. For before a man flee with a
Sparowhawke, she should be thoroughly well reclaimed, by watching, carrying,
feeding, and pluming before people, that she love her keepers fiste, and his
countenance, that she can abide both horses and dogs, that she be clean
within, as well skowred with washed meat, as also with plumage, and thaf she
be sharp set, and well coming, as well from the pearche, as from of the
ground, or out of a tree. ()

The mean to make a Sparowhawke fleeing.
depiction of sparrowhawk

First, he that would flee with a Sparowhawke lately reclaymed, must flee
in an evening somewhat before Sunnesette: For at that time she will be most
eager and sharpest set. Secondarily, the heat of the Sun (if one should
flee in the morning) does much trouble the hawke, and rayseth and stirs
her courage, making her proud and ramage. So that she leeseth the eagerness
of her appetite, and remembers it not, thinking on nothing else but to soar
and gadde abroad, whereby she may be easily lost. Furthermore, toward the
evening she cannot soar so far away from you, (although she should soar) as
she would do in the heat of the day, because the night will enforce her to go
to the perch and stand. Also to enter your Sparowhawke, it shall be best to
seek out some champaygne country, far from the wooddes, and let her be
vnhooded when the Spaniels be uncoupled, then if the Partridge spring, and she
bate, cast her off, if they spring near you. And if she kill, reward her (upon
the ground) of the head, brains, neck, and breaste of the Partridge. When
she has fed, take it from her, and vnseaze her, and get upon your horse a
good way from her. Then whistle and call her, and if she come unto you,
reward her better. Above all things, you must take good heed that she fail
not her first flyghte at great birds, least she turn tail, and accustom
her self to smaller game. But if she be once well entered at great game, you
may quickly make her flee Larkes and small birds. If you find that she have
most mind to flee Larkes, let her flee them, and reward her on them. For
there is no flyghte pleasaunter than the flyghte of the Sparowhawke at the
Lark. And for as much as the flesh and blood of Larkes is hot and
burning, it shall be good when you flee the Lark, to give your hawke washte
meat twyce in a week, and plumage very often. But give her no plumage that
day that she has washte meat, nor the day that she batheth. When there is
a knotte of good company met together, and every man has his Sparowhawke,
if one of the^ see his Sparowhawke flee when another is also from the fiste,
there begins the pastime, and yet they may flee together. But it is a
pleasure to take a Lark lowering, or clyming. Or if a Sparowhawke have beaten
down a Lark, or that ye Lark be slipte from her, This flight is not used in
England, neither did I ever hear of it before.
and gotten up towering as high as a man may behold, and then an other
Sparowhawke clime after her, get so high that by often taynting her, so as the
Lark is constrained to stowpe to the ground, and the hawke in the tail of
her. Then the Lark had rather flee for succoure between the legs of the men
and the horses, than to fall in the tallauntes of her natural enemy: yet
commonly she is there taken also. He that would learn to make a Falcon
well, let him begin with a Hobbie, and he that would make the Gerfalcon
flying, let him acquaynte himself with the Merlyne. But he that can keep and
make a Sparowhawke well, shall also be able to keep a Goshawke, for by the one
that other is learned.

To take lyme from of a hawks feathers.
TO remove the lyme from a hawke, take dry and fine sand and clean ashes
mingled together, put them upon the place which is lymed, and suffer it so
one night. Afterward you shall beat well together three yolks of eggs, and
with a feather you shall lay them upon the said places, and suffer the^ so
two nights. Then take as much larde as a plumme. and as much butter with it,
melt them both together, and anoynte the said places, and suffer the hawke so
one other night. Then on the morrow wash her with warm water, and wype her
with a clean lynnen clothe, until you have wyped of all the lyme, which by
this devise will easily be removed.

How to right and make straight bruised feathers.
IF you would make sound a bruised feather, then temper yt place which is
bruised, in warm water, and when the web thereof is well softened, and becomme
tender with the hot water, set it as even as you can out of the water.
Afterward take a great stalk of a Colewoorte, and warm it well upon the
coals, or in the flame: then cleave it in sunder, and within the clyfte put
the bruised feather, strayning the two sides of the Colewoorte stalk together,
until it have brought the bruised feather into his former estate. The stalk
of the herb called Brionie, has the same virtue.

To right and mende a feather broken on the one side, and to ympe a broosed
feather.
TAke a slender long needle, lay it in Vineager, or salt water, that it may
ruste and so hold the better within the feather: Afterward threade it with
untwisted threade, and draw it through both ends of the bruised places, then
draw it back by the threade, until it may draw that one part to that other,
so as the web may be close joined together: and suffer not your hawke to
flee, nor to use her wings, until it be closed and strong again. But if it
were broken on both sides, cut it off, and take a square ymping needle, like
unto a Glouers needle, lay it in Vineager and salt water, and thruste it into
both the ends of the web, until you have brought them together: then give
your hawke rest until the needle be rusted in that web. For a feather that
is broken or bruised within the quyll, take another quill that is lesser, that
it may go into the broken or bruised quyll, then cut off the feather in that
place, and the stalk of the quyll being put into the old quill, force the
end of the feather into the new quill that is cut: Afterward join
together the two pieces, with the quill that is so put in, covering the place
where it is so joined, with Cotton or small down feathers, with glewe or
Semonde, or if you would not ympe it, glewe it in with Semonde or Rosen and
wax molten together. If the feather be dropte away clean, then put in
another of like syze and color. For to bind in a feather that were slipped
out of the pynion, take flexe small chopped, and myngled with the yolke of an
egg well beaten, put them together upon a lynnen clothe, which is very near
worn, with the which you shall bind on both sides the place where the
feather slypped, or else anoint that place with Myrre and Goats blood myngled
together. To make a feather come again, which is cast and lost by bruise or
otherwise, and especially in the train of an hawke, take oil of Walnuttes,
and oil of Bays, as much of that one as that other, myngle them together,
and drop them into the place where the feather grew, and it shall put out a
new feather speedely. ()

How to bear and make a Falcon.
depiction of falcon

A Falcon lately taken, should be seeled in such sort, that when the
seeling begins to slacken, the Falcon may see forwards, to see the meat
before her. For she is better content when she sees the meat plainly
before her, than if she saw it sidewayes, or looking back. And she should
not be seeled too straight, neither yet ought the threede wherewith she is
seeled, be ouerstreyght bownde or knitte above her head, but twysted and
twyrled togythers. A hawke newly taken, should have new furniture, as new
Iesses, Leasse, and Bewettes, all of good leather, and the Iesses mayled, and
the Lease made with a button at the end. The must you have a little rownde
stycke hanging in a little string, with the which you shall oftentimes stroke
and handle your Falcon. For the more she is handled, the better she will be
manned, and becomme the gentler, and the more familiar: and also if you should
stroke or handle her with your hand, you might chance to catch a knappe of
her beak now and then. She must have two good belles, to the end she may
the better be found and heard, when she stirs or scratteth. She must have
a hoode of good leather, well made and fashioned, well raised and bossed
against her eyes, deep, and yet straight enough beneathe, that it may the
better abide on her head, and yet never hurt her. So must you also a little
cope her beak, and tallans, but not so much that you make them bleed.

How you shall manne a Falcon, and bring her out of her ramagenesse.
SOme say, that the soar Falcon which has been tymely taken, and has
already passed the seas, is both the best Falco^, and also the hardest to be
won and ma^ned. Wherefore observing the order which is before rehearsed, you
must feed such a Falco^ with good and warm metes (as pigeo^s, and such like
quick birds) until she be full gorged twice a day for three days. For you
must not break her of her accustomed diet all at once, and being lately
taken, she will more willingly feed on warm meats, than any thing else.
When you feed her, you must whoope and lewre as you do when you call a hawke,
that she may know when you will give her meat. You must vnhoode her
gently, gyuing her two or three byttes, and putting on her hoode again, you
must give her as much more. But take heed that she be well and close
seeled, three days being passed, if you perceive her to be eager and greedy
of meat, and that she feeds with good appetite, then begin to abate her
her meat, that is to say, give her but little at once, and often, so that
she have not much above at one time, until it be evening, and bear her
late upon your fiste before you go to bed, setting her upon a trestle or
stool very near you, so that you may wake her often in the night.
Afterward you should take her on your fiste again before day, with some
quycke bird or such like meat: and when you have observed this order with
her two or three nyghtes, and that you perceive she begins to be much
better fellow than she was wont, and that she seems to begin to be
reclaymed, and feeds eagerly upon good meat, then begin to change her
dyete, gyuing her often, and little at ones, the heart of an Hog or a Sheep.
In the evening when it begins to be late, (without casting of her) let her
seeling threade a little loose, spowting water on her face, that she may
Ieouke the less, and watching her all the night, hold her upon your fiste
vnhooded. But if she see any thing that muslike her, and make semblaunte to be
afearde, then let her be carried into some dark place, where you have no
more but light to hoode her again. And afterward give her some beaching of
good meat, and let her be watched diverse nyghtes together, until she be
reclaimed and Ieouke upon the fiste by day time, although to let her Ieouke
also sometimes in the night, is a thing which makes her the sooner manned.
In the morning by break of the day, let her have some warm meat to
begin with. And because there be sundry Falcons of sundry sorts and
conditions, (as some mewed at large in the wooddes, some other taken at stand,
where they have long used, and some other taken soar hawks, (whereof we now
treat) whither they be Sorehawkes, mewed or Nyasse, yet are they of sundry
natures and properties, and therefore they must be diversely governed and
entered, which is the cause that it is hard to give general rules. For those
which are gentle, easy to be reclaymed, and of a good kind and nature,
ought also to be the more favored, and the more gently handled. But when your
hawke is brought to the point before rehearsed, as well for the hooding, as
also for her eagerness to feed, if you perceive that she begin to be
acquaynted therewith, you may vnhoode her by day time, far from company,
first gyuing her a bytte or two of good meat. Afterward hoode her again
gently, gyuing her a little meat again hooded. Above all things, you must
beware to hoode or vnhoode her in any place, where she may be frayed, for
that were able to mar her at the first. When she begins to be
acquaynted with company, if you perceive that she be eager or sharp set,
vnhoode her, and give her a bitte or two of meat, holding her right against
your face and countenance. For that will cause her to dread no company. And
when it is night, cut the thredde wherewith she is seeled, and you shall not
need to watch her, if you perceive her bold enough amongst company. But
yet let her be set upon a trestle by you, that you may awake her two or
three times in the night, and take her on your fiste before day. For
ouerwatching of a hawke is not good, as long as a man may reclayme her
otherwise. And if by such good gouernmente, and by dealing curteousely with
her, and keeping her from sudden fear, you perceive that she begin to be
acquaynted with you, and to know you assuredly, and that she feeds eagerly,
and sticks to her meat before company, then give her washte meat, and
beache her in the morning, so that she may always have somewhat in her
gorge: which meat you shall lay in clear water half a day, and you shall
cause her to feed in company, giving her in the morning about Sun rising
the wing of a Hen or Pullette, and at evening, hooding her again, take the
foot of a Conie or an Hare, which is cut off above the ioynte, and flaye
it, stripping away the claws also, and temper and steep the skin in fair
water, (pressing and wringing it a little) the which you shall give her with
the ioynte of the pynion of a Hens wing. You must take good heed, how you
give your hawke any feathers, until she be thoroughly reclaimed. For until
she be thoroughly won and reclaymed, she dares not cast upon the fiste.
And on the fiste you must bear her continually, till she be thoroughly
manned. But when she makes semblante to cast, vnhoode her gently by the
tassell of the hoode. You may give her two days washte meat, and the third
day plumage, according as she is clean or foul within. And when she has
