cast, then hoode her again, gyuing her nothing to feed on, until she gleame
after her casting. But when she has cast and gleamed, then give her a
beaching of hot meat, in company gyuing her two or three byttes at once:
and at evening make her plume a Hens wing in company also. When you find
her well reclaymed, and thoroughly manned, and eager, and sharp set, then it
is time to feed her upon the lewer. And you must mark whither the feathers
of your hawks casting be foul or slymie, and whither the slyme thereof be
yellowe or not. For if they be, you must be very circumspect to make her
clean with washte meat and casting, and if she be clean within, then give
her not so strong castings, as Hares feet, or Connyes feet: but give her the
pynions of an old Hens wing, or the plumage that is to be taken thereupon,
or the neck bone chopped four or five times between the joints, washed, and
steeped in fair water. To make an end of this chapter, it is certain, that
it requires more time to win and to watch a Falcon once mewed in the
woodde, than one which is taken sore at passage. And likewise it is harder to
win a hawke taken at stand, when she has long time been accustomed, than it
is to make a hawke which has been handled before. ()

How to lure a Falcon lately manned.
depiction of falcon

BEfore you show the lewre to a Falcon newly reclaymed, you must consider three
things, First, that she be well assured and boldened in company, well
acquainted also with Dogs and with Horses. Secondarily that she be sharp
set and eager, having regard to the hour of the morning or evening when you
will lewre her. And the third consideration is, that she be clean within, the
lewre must be well garnished with meat on both sides, and you must be apart in
some secret place when you would give her the length of the lease. You must
first vnhood her, giving her a bit or twain upon the lewre, as she sits on
your fiste, afterward take the lewre from her, and hide it that she see it
not, and when she is vnseazed, cast the lewre so near her that she may catch
it within the length of her lease, and if she do seize upon it, then shall you
use the voice and accustomed speech of a Falconer unto his Hawke, and feed
her upon the lewre on the ground, gyuing her thereupon the warm thigh of an
Hen or Pullet, and the heart also. When you have so lewred her at evening,
give her but a little meat, and let her be lewred so timely, that when she is
therewith accustomed, you may give her plumage and a Iuk of a joint.
Afterward, and in the morning betimes, take her on your fiste, and when she
has cast and gleamed, give her a little beaching of good warm meat.
Afterward when the day is further forwards, and that it is time to feed
her, take a Criance and tie it to her lease, and go into some fair pleasant
meadowe, and give her a bitte or two upon the lewre, as before said, then
vnseaze her, and if you perceive that she be sharp set, and have seized upon
the lewre eagrely, then give her to hold unto some man which may let her off
to the lewre, then shall you unwind the Criance, and draw it after you a good
way, and he which holds the Hawke must hold his right hand on the tassell
of the Hawks hood in a readiness, that he may vnhood her as soon as you
begin to lewre, and if she come well to the lewre, and stoop upon it roundly,
and seize it eagerly, let her feed two or three bittes upon it, and then
vnseaze her, and take her from off the lewre, and hood her, and then deliver
her again to him which held her, and go further off and lewre her, feeding
her always upon the lewre on the ground: and using the familiar voice of
Falconers as they cry when they lewre. And thus you shall lewre her every day
further and further off, until she be well taught to come to the lewre, and
to take it eagrely.

Afterward let her be lewred in company, having regard that neither Dogs
nor other thing come in suddaynly to fray her, and when you take her up from
the ground, hood her upon the lewre, and when you have well and often lewred
her on foot, then use to lewre her on Horseback: the which you shall the
easlyer win her too, if when you lewre her on foot, you cause some on
Horseback to come near you, that she may see them, and cause them to come
near her when she feeds upon the lewre, causing them also to turn and
toss their Horses about her, but let their Horses be ruly, least they should
upon the suddayne affright her.

Furthermore the better to acquaint her with horses, and that she may the
better know them, carry your Falcon (whiles she feeds) on high upon the
lewre near unto some man on Horseback, or get your self up on Horseback,
and reward her upon the lewre amongst horsemen, and when she is well
accustomed to them and well acquaynted with them, making no resemblance to
fear them, you may then lewre her on horseback in this manner: He which
holds her to let her come to the lewre must be on foot, and you where you
lewre shall be on Horseback, and when you call and cast the lewre about your
head, then he which holds her, shall take off her hood by the tassell, and
you the mean while shall call and lure in the counningest wise you can, as
Falconers use to do: and if she sear eagerly upon the lewre, and fear neither
people nor horses, then take off the lewring line or Creance, and lewre her
loose further and further off. And to make a Falcon come which is but newly
reclaymed, and to make her come in company of another Hawke, there must be two
to hold a cast of Falcons, and two which shall lewre them, but he which
holds the Falcon that is but lately lewred, shall not let her come so soon
as the other shall do: then shall the lewre be thrown out unto the Falcon
which is but lately lewred, and when she is fallen thervppon, her keeper shall
carry her upon the lewre, to feed amongst the other make Hawks. This being
done twice or thrice, she will follow them and love them: and if you would
have her love dogs which is most necessary, you must call dogs about you
when you feed her, or give her tyring or plumage.

How you shall bathe your Hawke being but lately reclaymed, how you shall make
her fleing, and to hate the cherke.
WHen your Falcon is well lewred as well on horseback as on foot, and that she
is ready to be cast off, and has been well rewarded upon the lewre, and is
now altogether reclaymed from her ramage toys, and when she is also somewhat
recovered of the pain and travail which you have put her unto in making and
reclaiming her, and be yet in good plight, and have her thighs ploompe and
well brawned, then offer her water to bathe her, spy out a fair day when the
weather is clear and temperate, then take a basyn so deep that your Hawke may
stand therein up to the thighs, and fill it with water, and set it in some
secret place: afterward your Hawke being lewred and well rewarded in the
morning with warm meat, bear her up unto some high place, or bank, and
there hold her in the sun until she have endued her gorge, taking off her
hood, that she may proyne and pick her self: that being done, hood her
again, and set her near to the basin, afterward taking off her hood again
(if she will) let her leap down into the basin or upon the grass by it, and
to make her know the water, flappe therein with a little wand, and let her
bathe therein as long as she list: when she comes out of the water, take some
meat in thy hand and proffer it unto her, and be well ware that she come not
out before thou proffer her thy fiste to give her a bit or twain, then take
her up and hold her in the Sun, and she will pick and proyne her on your
fiste, or upon your: knee if she will not bathe her in a basyn, then proffer
her to bathe in river water at some foord. Bathing gives an Hawke great
courage, much boldness, and eager appetite: that day that she batheth, give
her nowasht meat. To make a new lewred Falcon, and to make her vpwardes, the
morrow after she has bathed, get on horseback in the morning or in the
evening, when she is sharp set, and choose out some field or pasture, where
fewest dooues or choughes be, then take your lewre well garnished on both
sides, and having vnhooded your Hawke, give her a bit or twain upon the
lewre, then taking it away from her, hood her again, then going fair and
softly against the wind, vnhood her: and before she bate or find any check in
her eye, whistle her from off your fiste fair and gently, and when she flies
about, (trotting forwards with your horse) cast our your lewre, and suffer her
not long to flee about you at the first. Continue this both morning and evening
for a few days, and if you perceive that your Hawke have no great list to
flee about you, nor to stoop to the lewre, and that she makes no semblaunce
to love other Hawks, then must you make her flee with one which loves other
Hawks, and which will not gadde out to any change or check: and that must
first be done at the partridge, for they flee not far before an Hawke: and if
your Falcon have flowen, and return to you twice or thrice, cast out the
lewre unto her, and reward her upon your Horseback, and afterward feed her
up, on the lewre upon the ground with good hot meat, to make her resolute
in her fleing, and that she may return to you with the better will, and if
the foul which you flew unto be killed by any other Hawke, let your Hawke
feed with the other Hawke, and when she is so rewarded a little, take her off,
and feed her upon the lewre.

If you flee to the river with your Falcon, and that the flight be fair and
likely to be landed, stay, and draw under the wind, and taking off your Hawks
hood, cast her off with the rest. When you would have your Hawke prove upwards
and a high flying Hawke, you must let her flee with a very high fleeing Hawke,
but see that your Hawke be well taught to hold in the head, and that she love
well to flee with the other Hawks, and if the foul be in a poole, or on a
pit or plash, you must first cast off the high fleeing Hawke, and he which
holds your new lewred Hawke, shall do well to get him under the wind, and
when he sees his vantage let him vnhood her, and if she bate then it is to
get up to the other Hawke. Then let him cast her off and she will climb
against the wind right unto the high fleeing Hawke, and before she weery
hirselfe too much with climbing to reach or cover that other Hawke, lay out
the foul, when the high fleeing Hawke shall be at her pitch, and lay them out
behind her, if she kill the foul, then give your Hawke reward of the Heart
and the breast with the other Hawks. If your Hawke go out to any check, and
kill a Dooue or a Crow, or any other check and feed upon it, or have fed
upon it before you come at her, seem not roughly to rebuke her at first, but
take her down to the lewre, giving her a bit of meat, and hood her up, and
flee not with her in two or three days after, but when you do flee, flee as
near as you can where there be no check, but if by no means you can keep
her from checking and going out, then for your last remedy, do as follows.
If your Hawke have killed a cheek, and you come to her before she have fed
thereon, take the gall of a Hen, and anoynte therewith the breast of the
cheek which she has killed, when she has plumed it and is come to the
blood, and let her feed but little thereon, least you make her sick, for she
will surely cast it again, yea though she should not cast it, yet would she
have small lust to flee at such a foul again, but will hate and loath the
meat thereof, or put any bitter thing therevppon, as powder of mirre, or young
small woormes cut in gobbets, but take heed that the bitterness be not over
strong, and if that the bitter taste have discouraged the Hawke, then weate her
meat in sugared water. Some put on two pair of belles upon their Hawks
legs, or stitch together their principal long feathers of their wings, and
also it shall be good when she goes from cheek, to cast her out the lewre, or
to lay out an hurt foul before her which she may kill. ()

How to flee the Hearon.
depiction of men, one on horseback, with dogs and birds

TO make your Falcon a good Hearoner, you must set her very sharp, and have a
live Hearon, whereof you shall make your Hawke a quarrey in this sort. In the
morning when it shall be time to feed your Hawke, if you perceive that she be
very sharp set, go to a meadow, and let the Hearon go after that you have
bruised both his feet and his bill, and hide your self behind some bush: and
then he which holds the Hawke shall vnhood her, the which shall be under the
wind. And if your Hawke will not flee at the Hearon, cast out your lewre the
which you shall hold in a readiness therefore: but if she do seize on the
Hare, make her a quarrey thereon, giving her first the Heart, and when she has
eaten it, give the Hearon to him which held the Hawke before, who retiring
back a little shall lewre, tossing the Hearon about his head, holding her by
one of the legs or wings: then do you vnhood your Hawke again, and let her
flee to him which lewreth sowith the Hearon, and let him not cast it out unto
her but stay until she take and seize it in his hand as he lewreth with it:
then despoyle the breast of the Hearon and let your Hawke feed upon it, and
take ye marrow of the bone in the Hearons wing, and give it your Hawke: and in
this doing two or three days you shall nowsel your Hawke thereunto and make
her love the Hearon, the which you shall also bring the sooner to pass, if at
ye first you inure her with a make Hawke a good Hearoner. Then having found the
Hearon at siege, you must get you with your Falcon up into some high place,
into the wind, and let him which has the Hearoner (that is the male Hawke) put
up the Hearon, and when he has cast off his Hawke to her, let him mark
whether the Hearon do mount or not, for if she mount, then cast not off your
Hawke, nor vnhood her not, but if the Hearon seem to be discomfited, and that
she fall down into the water, and that the make Hawke do stoop her, then
vnhood your young Hawke, and advance her: if she bate to be gone, let her flee
to it.

How a man shall make his Hawke to love other Hawks, when she hates to flee
with them.
THere are some Falcons which will not flee with other Hawks, but draw
backward, and stir not: some other will crabbe with every Hawke, and flee of
purpose to crabbe with them: some Falcon hates to sit or to flee with
another Hawke, either for doubt and fear which she has of them, or else for
because she loves the^ not. That hawke which hates other Hawks does crabbe
with them, and she which fears them, does flee from them. For remedy
hereof you must have a gentle Lanner, which may be set on a pearche with
that Hawke which hates others, but far enough off, and by day light, then
give each of them a bitte of meat as you pass by them, and set them nearer
and nearer, and when they be near one to another, put meat between them
that both of them may feed upon it. Then if the Falcon make no semblant to
crabbe with the Laner, you shall gorge her up at night with good meat, and set
her abroad in the frost or cold upon a pearche, if she be high and in good
plight able to abide it, and so shall you let her sit three or four
hours. In the mean time hold your Lanner near to the fire, and afterward
take her upon your fiste, then let another bring you your Falcon hooded, and
hold her close between your side and the Lanner, and when she feels the
warmth of the Lanner, she will draw to her, and hug to her for the heat,
and let them stand so together without ieouking (either the one or the
other) until you see that the Falcon do greatly desire to ieouke, then
vnhoode her fair and softely, and let it be in such a place as she see not,
but let her sit so all the night upon your fiste. And when day appears, you
must set them on the pearche that one near to the other, yet so as they cannot
one reach to another: That being done two or three nights together, let
them both be set abroad the third night in the cold, so near that they may
sit close together on the pearche, and when you see them sit close that one to
that other for warmth, then vnhood them, and afterward feed them, pearche
them, and lewre them both together, and take pain to find the advantage.

How you shall enseame a Hawke, or give her castings, skourings. etc.
SOme Falcons be harder to enseame, than some others are, for the longer that a
Falcon has been in the hand, the harder she is to be enseamed: and an old
mewed Falcon of the wood, which has mewed but one cote in the Falconers
hands, is much easyer to be enseamed, than a younger Falcon which has been
longer in the Falconers hands: the reason is, because a Hawke which prays
for her self, does feed cleaner, and better, according to her nature, and
upon more wholesome meats, than she does when she is in man's hands, so that
it is no marvel though she be not so foul within when she is at her own
diet, as when another man feeds her. For a Hawke which is in our keeping
does feed gredily both on skin, feathers and all that commes to hand.
Neither is she mewed with so clean and wholesome feeding, nor does endue her
meat so well, nor has such open ayree at times convenient as a Hawke which
is at large to pray for her self. When you draw your Hawke out of the mewe, if
she be greasy (the which you shall know by her thighs if they be round
and fat, and also by her body if she be full in the hand, and that her flesh
be round as high as her breast bone) and if she be well mewed, and have all
her feathers full sommed, then give her when she would feed in the Morning a
bitte or two of hot meat, and at night give her but a little, unless it be
very cold, and if she feed well without constreynt or forcing thereunto, then
give her washed meat thus prepared: take the wings of a Hen or Pullet for her
dinner, and wash them in two waters, and if you give her Hares flesh or Beef,
let it be washed in three waters, on the morrow give her the leg of a Hen
very hot, and at noone meat temperately warm, a good gorge, then let her
fast until it be late in the evening, and if she have put over her meat and
that there be nothing left in her gorge, then give her a little warm meat, as
you did in the morning, and let her thus be dietted until it be time to give
her plumage, the which you shall know by three tokens, the first is, that
feeling the end of the pinion of the Hawks wing, you shall feel the flesh as
it were tenderer, and softer than it was before she eat washed meat: the
second, if her mewtes be clean and white, and that the black of the mewt be
right black and not mingled with any other foul thing or color: the third
token is, if she be very sharp set and do plume eagerly, you may give her
casting either of a Hares foot or a Coonnies foot, or of the small feathers,
which are on the joint of the pinion of an old Hens wing: take then the
forefoote of a Hare, and beat it with the back of a knife until the bones
and claws do fall out, because the small bones may mouldre and be all to
frushed to pieces, the which you shall afterward cut and lay it in fair
fresh water, then wring it, and give her it at two morsells, and when you set
her on the pearche, sweep clean underneath it that you may see whether the
mewte be full of strakes or skins or not, and whether it be full of slime
and ordure or not, and if it be, then continue this kind of casting three or
four nights together with washed meat as is before said, and if you perceive
the feathers disgested and soft, and that her casting be great and full of
ordure, then take the neck of an old Hen, and cut it all along between
the joints; and lay the joints in clear cold water, and give it to your Falcon
without any other thing: and this is done because it bears down before it
into the pannell, the meat which is upon the joints, and there in the pannell
the flesh co^sumeth and the bones remain sharp and pricking, which break the
kelles and skins, and the gross ordure, and bear them with them: and give
her so three nights together, giving her by day washed meat as is before
rehearsed, and afterward give her casting or plumage again, according as her
state does require. And think it not strange that a Falcon which is to be
enseamed, is sometimes a fortenight or more before she will take casting:
neither yet that some Falcons will easiyer be enseamed in a month, than some
other in five weeks, according as they be stronger or weaker of nature, and
fed with cleaner or fowler meats, or according as they have bin shorter or
longer time in men's hands and keeping. When you have drawn your Falcon out
of the mewe, and that her principal feathers be full sommed, or that she have
some yet in the quill, do not give her washed meat, but quick birds, and good
gorges thereof, and set her as much as may be in open places, for otherwise
her feathers may chance to shrink in the quill, and come to nothing.

To enter or make a Hawke, after the fashion of Lombardy.
WHen a Sparowhawke is ma^ned and reclaymed, then give her nine or ten traynes
at the least, and when she kills, feed her up always, and let the Quayle
wherewithal you train her, have a feather pulled out of each wing, and cast
off the Sparowhawke to her a far off, so often yt she will recover the Quayle
far off, and then cast her out a Quayle which has her full wings, afterward
you may flee the wild Quayle with her: and evermore when she does kill, feed
her up with a full gorge: the Almaines are of opinion that the tercell of the
Goshawke is more weighty and more valiant than the Hawke, both to Partridge
and Feasant. If you would make a Sparowhawke to the Pie, then dismember the
Pie, and cast her on the ground to the Hawke, and feed her upon her with a
hot meal, as with a Finche or such like bird, and use that order with her
twice or thrice, and afterward you may cast up a Pie to your Hawke that is
seeled, being dismembered as beforesayd, and let her kill her, and feed her
upon her, also you may take a feather or two out of the Pyes wing, and set her
up in some tree, and let your Hawke kill her there, and make her as good reward
as you can thereof, and this being thus used, you may flee with your Hawke to
the wild Pie, but ever remember that in making these trayns your Pie be
disme^bred that is to say, her bill and her tallents cut off, or so tied and
abated that she may not spoil or hurt your Hawke. Goshawkes and Tercells of
Goshawkes are better when they are taken haggards of a Cote or two Cotes out of
the wood, than they are when they be Sorehawkes, but then they must be kept
with more delicate feed then the Soarehawkes, for they are made dangerous.
For as much as in the wooddes they did commonly pray upon warm meats, and
therewithal they are sooner lost than Sorehawkes be, by reason they remember
their eyree, but they should not be above one or two Cotes at the most. ()

To enseame a Falcon and to make her.
depiction of falcon

TAke your Falcon and use her as you use the Goshawke in manner before declared,
saving that when you feed her you shall call and lewre as if you called her to
the lewre: and every day you shall proffer her water, and every night give her
casting accordingly as you shall see that she endeweth: and take off her hood
often times amongst company, and to the end she shall not bate, hold alwais the
hood by the tassell in your hand ready. And in the evening when daylight
begins to fail, take off her hood amongst company of people by the
candlelight until she rowze and mewte, and then set her on ye perch and not
before, and set light before her, and when she is well woonne to know the
fiste, then begin to make her know the lewre, and so by little and little
reclayme her, until you may call her loose without criance. Every Falcon has
need of a make Hawke to teach her to hold in the head, and especially the
haggart Falcon, the which may be peradventure an haggart of two or three cotes,
and yet shall be the better Hearoner: but if a haggart mewed will not hold in
the head, then cut off some part of her two principalles in each wing, the
long feather and that which is next to it, and that shall force her to hold in.
You must also feate her beak and cope her reasonably. They always give their
Hawks tyring both morning and evening, but the Falconers of the East parts are
of a contrary opinion and say that it weakeneth a Hawks back. If you would
make your Hawke upwards or high flying, then after she is reclaymed and lewred
and ready coming, when you lewre her and that she comes toward you, stop
ye lewre upon her and let her pass by you, and when she retourneth toward you
throw her out the lewre, and make much of her, and do thus oftentimes until
you may suffer her to flee longer about you and to get up higher, the which
you must do in a plain field where no wood or trees are: and if she get up to
any pitch, then let her flee a turn or two of iollity, and when she is at the
highest and right over you, throw her forth the lewre, or a Pigeon or Pullet,
and give her a good gorge thereon, making the most of her that you can devise,
and take heed that you cast not your lewre into the water, least she should
thereby be rebuked. And when she is at her gate, if percase she gadde out after
some check, and kill it, then take the pray from her angerly, and beat her
therewith about the head, and hoode her up without any reward: and hereby she
will the less delyght to rake out after a check. When your hawke has killed
a foul, take it out of her foot, and cast her up again, and when she is
right over you, cast out the lewre, and feed her up upon it, to make her love
the lewer the better. But at the beginning reward her and feed her well upon
the quarrey, and that shall encourage her and keep her from going out to the
check. When she is well in blood, and well quarried, then let her flee with
other hawks, until she be well acquainted and inured.

If you would make your hawke to the Crane, take a Nyasse Falcon gentle, and in
entering of her let her kill the greatest foul that you can find. Her lewre
should also be a cou^terfayte Crane. And when you would make her fleeing,
let her flee from the fiste, and succor her quickly. For you must have
dogs made for the purpose, which may help and succoure her sooner than a
man can do. And let such dogs feed always with your hawke, to make them
the better acquaynted. If you would make a Falcon to the Hare, her lewre
should be then a Hares skin stuffed with straw, and when she is well lewred,
and that you would enter her, tie the said Hares skin to the end of a
cryance, and fasten it to your saddle pommell, and when you galloppe, it will
be like unto a running Hare. Then vnhoode your hawke, and cry, Back with the
Greyhoundes, back with the Greyhoundes. And when your hawke comes to seize
the said Hares skin, let go your cryance, and suffer her to take it, and
reward her well upon it, and make the most of her that you can devise. And when
you go about to enter her ye second time, let not slyppe your cryance at the
first, but rather pull it from her by force, and afterward let her seize
upon it, and so by little and little you shall teach her to beat it and stowpe
at it. For so must she do at a wild Hare. And you must feed her always
amongst the dogs, and when she is well nousled and entered in this manner,
take a live Hare, and break one of her hinder legs, and let her go in some
fair place amongst your dogs, and your Falcon will stowpe her and ruff
her, until the dogs may take her. Then take her from the dogs, and cast
her out unto the hawke, and cry back, back.

If you would make your hawke fleing to the Partridge, or Feasant, when she is
reclaimed and made, then every time that you lewre her, cast your lewre into
some low tree or bushe, that she may learn to take the tree, or the stand.
And if she take the stand before she epsy the lewre, let her stand a
while. And after draw the lewre out before her, and cry, Lo bird, lo, Hey lo
bird, hey lo, and reward her well. For in this manner she will learn to take
stand. But feed her always on the ground, and in some thick place, for in
such places she must encounter with the Feasant at pearche. And at the first
flee with her at Feasant, or Partridge that be young, to give her the
advantage, and afterward at the old. If a Falcon will not take stand, but
will keep her on wing, then must you flee with her in plain places, where
you may always see her upon you. Sacres and Laners will commonly take stand
both in a tree, and on the ground, but the Falcon Gentle takes stand more
willingly upon the ground. And when you draw a hawke out of the mewe, bear
her not much in hot weather, for fear least by much bating in heat, she
may get the Pantyse. But if there be no remedy, then keep her always
hooded, and take as good heed to her as you can. If your hawke be coye and
dangerous, then give her salt with her meat, I mean a dram of the Salt
called Sal Inde, or Sal geme, or Salis albi pulueri-Zati, and give her water,
for she shall have need thereof. And make her Ieouke all night in pain, and
in a moist or cold place, and so shall she watch most of the night, and
thereby her grease and pride will be abated. Sacres must be nowsled and entered
as soon as they be manned, for else they are very hard to be entered. Draw
your hawke out of the mewe twenty days before you enseame her. If a Falcon
trusse and carrye, to remedy the same, you must cope her tallantes, her
powlse, and her petie single. Never reward your hawke upon river foul, but
reward her, and make much of her upon the lewre, to the end she may the
better know it and esteem it. The Souldane flees to the Crane, wild Goose,
and Bustarde, with three or four hawks at once (or more) from the fiste, yea
and those of all kind of hawks also, as Sacres, Gerfalcons, peregrine
Falcons, and Vilanes. And afterward a man may make them flee to the mowntie.
You should flee to the Crane before Sun rising, for she is sluggishe and
slowthfull, and you may cast off to her a cast or a lease of Falcons, or you
may hawke to her with the Goshawke from the fiste without dogs. Wild geese
are flowen to in the same manner. And if you have dogs to help and succor
your hawks, see that they be stanche and gentle, and well made for the
purpose, and a Greyhounde will be most readily made thereunto. You shall flee
but once in a day at the Crane, and thereupon reward her liberally, and make
as much of her as you can. The Vyllaine should be let flee down the
wind. The Almaines do flee at the Pie with a lease or two cast of Falcons
at once, and they make them to mownte and to stowpe, as they do at the River.
But this must be in a plain, where there be no trees nor woodde, but little
shrubbes and bushes. If you use to give your hawke a brekefast or beaching very
timely in ye morning, it will make her eager to flee at such time as will be
convenient for her to flee. And especially a Falcon, which you would have
high fleeing, and those should not be highly kept, but should be fed nine
days together before Sun rising, and at night late in the fresh or cool
of the evening. So shall you make them high fleeing, and by that means they
will every day get gate higher and higher, so yt they be flowen with evermore
in the plain champaygne. The Falcon gentle is commonly better inward when she
has mewed, than in her soarage. The Falcon will kill the Hearne naturally, if
she be a peregrine Falcon, and yet you shall do well to give them traynes. A
Falcon may flee ten flightes at river in one day, if the season be not extreme,
and that is the most that you shall flee with her. The Falcons which are
river hawks, should always be borne upon the fiste. A hawke shall have forty
castings before she be perfectly made. For a hawke which has not casting
every night, will be surcharged with abundance and superfluity of humors, the
which do (from the stomach) so ouerflow their brain, that they cannot flee
so high as else they would do. And therefore all hawks should have casting
every night naturally, if you would have them sound and clean: and it is
good to give them tyring or plumage at night, especially field hawks, but not
river hawks, for weakening their backs. And every third day you shall
present them to the water at the longest. Touch your hawks feathers as little
as you can, for much handling will make them out of order. The Vyllaine and
the Lanerette may be set upon the stone incontinently, as soon as they be
made. When your hawke has flowen or bated, feed her not as long as she
pants, or blows, nor until she be in breath again. For if you should it
is perilous to bring her into the disease called Astma, and (in a hawke) the
Pantise. If a hawke (either Falcon or other) chance to be out of heart, and
discouraged, (which happens oftentimes) then take such pains with her that
she may kill some pray, and feed her up upon it with a full gorge even as
long as she will eat, and the same night set her abroad, that she may
Ieouke in the open air at her own pleasure: and on the morrow take her and
feed her with small birds to enseame her, neither more nor less than you
would do with an hawke that were new drawn out of the mewe. If a Falcon or
other hawke will not seize or gorge, take the quill of a wild Gooses feather,
and tie it under her long single, and she will seize and grype. And when she
begins to feaze, take away the said quyll, and she will seize long
afterward. If you cannot give covert to your Falcon, or your Goshawke, then
cast her off with the Sun in her back. All hawks may be made flee at ye
sowrce or spring. But in what sort soever you flee with your Goshawke, let
her have the Sun in her back.

To flee all manner of fowls.
ALl trains of Partridge, Rookes, Crows, and Choughs, should be seeled.
Now to enter your hawke at any of them, make a little pytte or hole in the
ground, and put your train therein. Then cover the hole with a little
board, the which you shall fasten to a small cryaunce or cord, and that you
shall hold in your hand to draw away when you list. The^ you shall make as
though you uncoupled your spanels to hunte and put up the game, and you shall
carry your hawke vnhooded. And when you perceive that your hawke looks that
way where you have laid the train, then draw of the board, and cause the
train to spring as though the spanels had sprung it. And if your hawke do take
it, let her feed thereon her fill upon the ground: And thus must you do
sundry times. If you would have a good hawke, then nowsel her young, for so
will she increase her force by little and little, and in the end, she will
ouercomme both Feasaunte and Partridge, etc. And when she has killed, let
her grype and seize the pray at her pleasure. And let her also plume
thereupon as long as she will, and evermore let her be rewarded upon the
ground. And when she is well nowsled, then reward her never but upon the
cocks of all prays, because that will make her love that pray the better.
And when she kills a hen of any kind, let her no more but plume upon
it, and give her but the heart, and the brain at the most, because she shall
not love to flee the Hen so well as the Cock. It is much better to nousel
hawks at young fowls, than at the old. For most commonly if a young hawke be
let flee at old game, she will turn tail, and cowardly give it over,
unless you do as before is declared. If you would nousel or enter a haggarde,
then do not enter her or set her in blood upon a young pray, or inure her
thereto. For then she would not afterward pass much for old game. And
likewise for the same consideration you shall not nousel nor enter a mewed
hawke at young game.

For the Goshawke all fowls generally are good traynes, as Crane, Bustarde,
Hearne, wild Goose, River foul, Cormorantes, Choughes, Rookes, Kyghtes, and
all other foul that haunt Ryuers or plashes.

How to make a train or flight for the Goshawke.
Set one of the said fowls upon the water, and between you and the water,
let there be some small shrubbes or bushes, so as the Goshawke may have
couerte to take the stand if need be, as also to keep her out of sight of
the foul, for her advantage. Then advance your fiste, so as the hawke may
discrie the foul. After which you may hold it lower again, and so cast off
the hawke. And if so be yt she seize the foul, let her feed thereon at her
pleasure on the ground. To make your Goshawke to the brook, let her flee
those traynes before said, as I have told you. But when you see the hawke
approach the foul, and to be within danger, then strike up your drum before
such time as ye foul do epsy your Goshawke. For if she once see the hawke
before she spring, she will by no means willingly forsake the brook, but
fall to dyuing and ducking, a defense which nature has provided and taught
the^.

Thus must you deal with the Goshawke to the brook, but if you will flee
with her to the Hare and Lyueret, (which is a game that the Goshawke does
much delyghte to kill and pray on) then must you breeche her, and at no time
let her flee without that devise, for fear least she spoil her self. For the
Hare is of some force, and in striving to escape from her, will force her to
stretche her arms, and open her self to wide, which is the utter undoing of
your hawke. The Sparowhawkes do use to kill the foul at the Sowrce or Souse,
as the Goshawkes do, which nature has taught them, (for that being round
winged hawks,) if they dealt not upon the advantage, the foul might easily
slip from them, and escape their danger. But so great is the courtesy of
kind, as she ever seeks to recompense any defect of hirs, with some other
better benefit, or at least such as shall serve the turn. Thus have you the
French Falconers opinions laid down, as touching the fleeing with each kind
of hawke, or at least, the greatest part of them. For the knowledge to flee
with the Falcon, serves for all Tower hawks, and the Goshawke for the round
winged hawks. ()

To manne, hoode, and reclayme a hawke, after the opinion of the Italian
Falconer.
depiction of man with falcon

HE that will furnishe his hawke accordingly, must have Iesses and Bewettes of
good leather, and shrill belles, according to the hugeness or condition of his
hawke. So must he also have a hoode for her: and therewithal he must
oftentimes hoode and vnhoode her, in such sort that she be not afraid
thereof, nor of her keeper when he handles her. In nine nights he should
not suffer her to iouke at all, nor to come on any perch, but should keep
her continually so long upon his fist. And when he will call her, let him
observe this order: Set the Falcon upon the perch and vnhoode her, then show
her your fiste with some meat in it, and call her so long till she come to it.
And when she comes feed her, and reward her as pleasauntly as you can: But
if she come not, give her nothing at all until she be very sharp set. And
this order must you keep with your Hawke seven or eight days together.

When you would lure her, give her unto some other man to hold, and call her
with a lure well garnished with meat on both sides, as you called her to your
fist. After you have used that manner of calling six days or thereaboutes,
cause her to be held further from you, and cast the lure about your head, and
throwing it out upon the ground a little from you: And if she come to it
roundly, then feed her and reward her bountifully. And whiles your hawke is
upon the lewre, go about her fair and softly, lewring and crying: woe ho ho,
as Falconers use. And when you have thus done by the space of certain days,
take your lewre garnished as before said, and every day call her to you as
far as she may well hear and perceive you: and let her be loose from all
her furniture, that is without either loins or cryaunce: and if she come so
far off to you, then feed and reward her well, and stop her in her feeding
oftentimes, for that will make her come the better: but take heed that you
hurt her not in so doing. You shall also sometimes call her on horseback. And
when you have used her thus a month, or until she come well and roundly, and
that she be familiar with the man without any strangeness or coyness, then
may you stop the lewre upon her, and make her flee upon you. But before you do
so, it shall be meet to bathe her, least when she is at liberty she rangle to
seek water, and so you might leese your Hawke: and every seven or eight days,
your Hawke should be set to the water, for the nature of them so requires.
When you have thus manned, reclaymed, and lewred your Falcon, go out with her
into the fields and whistle her of your fyst, standing still to see what she
will do, and whether she will rake out or not. But if she flee round upon you,
as a good Hawke should flee, then let her flee a turn or two: which done,
throw her out the lewre, and let her foot a hen or a pullet, and kill it,
and feed her well therevppon. Vnhoode her often as you bear her, and cease
not so to do until she have endewed and mewted sufficiently.

When your Falcon is thus made and manned, go abroad with her every morning
when the weather is fair and calm, and choose a place for her to flee in,
where there is some narrow brook or plashe of water. And when you cast her
off, go into the wind so far that the foul may not descrie you. And when
she is cast off, and begins to recover her gate, make you then toward the
brook where the foul lie, always wyling and making your Hawke to lean in
upon you. And when you perceive that she is at a reasonable pitche, then (her
head being in,) lay out the foul, and land it if you can, and if you cannot,
take down your Hawke, and let her kill some train, as thus. Take with you a
Ducke, and slip one of her wing feathers, and having thrust it through her
nares, throw it out unto your Hawke, and cast it as high as you can right
underneath your Hawke, that she may the better know your hand, and you. And
remember that you never flee a young Hawke without some quick thing carried into
the field with you, that if she fail at first to kill the wild foul, you may
yet make her kill that train which you bring with you. And this you shall do
for a certain time, until your Hawke be well entered and quarreyed, and that
she know a quarreyor sufficiently. Some other Falcons there be of a contrary
nature, which will require great skill to find their properties. And the same
being known, you may keep them high or poor, according to their conditions.
So shall you do more good with them, than if you have no respect to the
diversity of their nature: for then you should commit great errors, and
seldom make good Hawks. You may train Hawks in this wise. First you shall
feed your Hawke well upon a foul of the same kind that you would train her
withal, or have her flee to. And you shall do so until you have acquainted
your Hawke with that kind of foul, the which you may do in this sort. Take
that foul that you will make the train of, and set it on foot with meat
tied upon the back of it, and go so near it that the Hawke may see it: and
when she sees it, let her seize thereupon, and foot the foul and kill it. Or
you may thus do better: Take a Cryance, and tie the foul (which has the meat
tied on her back) by the beak, and cause one to stand close which may hold
the same Cryance. Then vnhoode your Hawke, and stand a far off, and let him
draw the foul and stir it with his Cryance, until your Hawke may see it
stir. And if she foot it, then may you afterward make her this other kind
of train. Take a quick foul which can flee, and when you have half seeled it,
and cast it out, let your hawke flee to it, and if she kill it, reward and feed
her up well upon it. ()

To keep and make Sparhawkes.
depiction of sparrowhawk

IF you would know how to feed and man Sparrowhawkes that be taken tender
penned out of their eyree, it is meet that you keep them in a fresh and
sweet place, and give them as much as they will eat, of small birds, as
Sparrowes, Martlets, and such like. And also you may give them other flesh, but
see that it be sweet, clean, and good, shred into small pellets upon a clean
trencher. When they begin to wax full somed, give them Sparrowes, and other
small birds whole, that they may learn to plume, foot, and tire: and set
them a Basin of clean water in a heap of sand, that they may bathe therein
and proine and pick their feathers. They that be thus dealt withal do not
know how to pray and therefore you must enter them by traynes in this manner.
Take a young chicke^ which is of color and plume like a Feasant or Partrich,
and cast it out before your Sparhawke. But if she flee not unto it, nor do
foot it, then strip the skin upon the head of the chicken until it bleed, and
she will lightly seize it, thinking that it is flesh to feed upon. Then feed
her upon it well, and thus you shall use her until she will seize a chicken of
hirself. When she will take a chicken of hirself, go into some fair close or
meadow where nothing may be to interrupt you, and take a young chicken and throw
it up unto your hawke until she flee to it and foot it: then feed her up
thereupon, and coy her as much as you can devise: For Nyasse Sparhawkes are
much more dangerous and coy than others be. When your Sparhawke will foot a
chicken well as before said, then you may train her thus. Cause some of your
servants or others to stand close in some ditche or other privy corner, with
a live chicken in his hand: and stand your self with your Sparhawke upon
your fist a little distance from him: then cry and speak as you would speak,
and cry to your Spaniels when they range the field. And when you think that
your Sparhawke looks that way as he that has the train stands, let him
cast up the chicken, as high as he can, and let your Sparhawke flee thereat,
and seize it. That done, reward her, and feed her thereupon, making much of
her, and cheering her evermore among. Thus shall you do twice a day. When you
have thus trained her, mount on your horseback, and give her like traynes in
the field as before said. Then set her sharp against an evening, and go out to
seek some game: and if you find, seek to make her one flight at advantage,
and let her flee but once, and suppe her up upon the pray. Thus in four or
five days at the beginning I would have you let her flee but one flight in a
day, rewarding her well always when she kills any thing, to the end she may
know her game the better, and take the greater delight therein, until she be
thoroughly nousled. To keep your Sparhawke in good order, rise early in the
morning, and taking her upon your fyst, tappe her on the train with your two
forefingers, and stroke on her wings that she may mantle and warble, and
advance her self bolt upright and delicately upon your fyst. Sometimes also
take off her hoode, and put it on again fair and softly, for rebuking her.
When you have kept her two hours upon the fist, then set her in the Sun to
weather her half an hour: that being done call her to your fist with meat,
and whistle to her often, and chirpe with your lips, that she may learn to
know that voice when you call her, and when she comes reward her well. Thus
you should use her daily, until she come well to the fyst: remembering always
that you deal with her as gently and as amiably as you can devise. When she is
well manned, and well coming, try if she covet not the water to bathe her.
And if she do bathe, then may you afterward flee with her: But first get on
horseback, and call her from off the ground with a Cryance, for fear least
she should think strange to come to you on horseback: and if she come well
to you from the ground, being on horsbacke, then may you boldly flee with her.
But remember always to epsy some flight with advantage at the first, for
discouraging of your Hawke: For if she be once well nousled and entered, you may
afterward he the bolder how and when to flee with her.

The manner to feed a Sparhawke.
TO feed a Sparhawke orderly, you should give her good meats, as thighs of
Chickens and Pullettes, young Sparrowes, Martelettes and other small birds.
Also sheep hearts, or Lambs hearts: all these be excellent meat for a
Sparhawke. And those you must give them clean and hot, if it be possible:
for with hot meats you shall keep her always in best plighte, and least
danger to abate. And to prevent diseases, you shall give her every night
casting of Cotton or Lint, or such like. And yet some are of opinion, that it
is not best to give a Sparhawke casting so often: But by their leave, I think
it meet: and my reason is, that when you give her casting, you shall always
perceive the disposition of her gorge and pannell from time to time, by the
signs which you shall see upon the casting, and in it: As sometimes it will
be white, sometimes black, sometimes yellow, sometimes green, and of other
colors: and by this means knowing the infirmity of your Hawke, you may the
better provide remedy for the same. Therefore I think it best to give a
Sparhawke casting, and it shall not be amiss to put therein sometimes a whole
Cloue, in a pellet of Cotton, which is a tried medicine to draw evil humors
from a Hawks head: Or sometimes you may put a chyue of Saffron in her
casting. And above all things, give her water once in three or four days: For
Sparhawkes do much covet the water, and it is the best thing (with other
good diet) that can be, to keep a Sparhawke always sound and in good case.

Of feeding a Hawke.
TAke heed that you feed not your Hawke with two sorts of meat at once, for
that is perilous. Forasmuch as the substaunces of them being diverse, they
strive in disgestion or endewing, and breed evil humors and worms in a
Hawke, and sill her with wind. And beware also that you feed not your Hawke
with flesh of beasts that be old, nor that be hurt or taynted: For such
meats are hard to endewe, and full of venom and corruption, breeding
diseases. Beware also that you give not your Hawke the flesh of a brood
hen. And it shall not be amiss sometimes to change your Hawks meat and
feeding.

Gooseflesh (if you use it oftentimes) will breed many flegmatike humors in a
hawke, and the cray in her pannell.

The flesh of a young Calfe is good for a gorge or two: but if you give it
oftener, These fedings are for the most part contrary to our english order in
Falconrie.
it ingendreth phlegm and cold humors in the head, and breadeth lise in her.
The heart is much better for common feeding than the rest.

The flesh of a Ram goat, a female goat, or a gelded goat, is good to set up a
hawke. But some are of opinion that it breeds the gout, and moist watrie
humors, and opilations in the gorge.

Mutton is not good, for it breeds humors in the head, as the Rye and such
like: it dries up and enflames a Hawke, and makes her hose sit close to her
legs.

Lambs flesh, and the flesh of a Kidde, is good given with Goats milk,
especially when your hawke is poor, and has been hardly dealt withal.

Hares flesh, or Conies flesh, either cold or hot, is very good to endew,
and sound meat for a Hawke: but take heed that you give her none of the
brains, nor any of the hairs or bones, for they are perilous, and breed
worms in the gorge and in the guts.

Cattes flesh is unwholesome, and hard to be indewed, and breeds perilous
worms, and stops a Hawke in the gorge, and marreth her wind.

Rattes flesh is good and wholesome: it consumes choler, helps to loose
the head, gives good appetite and disgestion.

Dogs flesh is good and very sound, and very meet for those kind of
Falcons, which are whote Hawks of nature.

Woolues flesh is naught, and contrary to a Hawks nature.

This feeding we use not.
The flesh of a Fox (as my Author affirms) is wholesome and sets up a
hawke.

If your Hawke happen to sit abroad in the cold air, and especially in the
night, give her small birds to feed upon, because they are hot of nature and
comfort much: but beware that you use it not continually, for it will make her
too stately, and cause her forget you, in such sort, that you shall hardly
reclayme her from her coyness.

To feed with river foul and such like, there are some of them good, and some
bad, as I will breefly declare.

Some hold opinion, that the bones and blood of a Bargander, Moorehen, and such
like, may be given to an hawk: but that the skin, flesh, or feathers of them
are not good, because they breed evil humors.

The flesh of a Ducke or of a Crane (as they report) is good and wholesome.

The flesh of a Partriche is most excellent when a Hawke is sick and diseased.

The flesh of a wild Goose, or of a flecked Crow, is good: but you must give
your Hawke but little thereof, and none at al of their blood: for it is salt,
brackish, and of an evil nourishment.

The flesh of these flesh Crows, and of the Wagtayles (a Dishwasher as we
term them, in Latin called Motacilla) and the Cormorant, is of evil
nourishment and disgestion.

The flesh of a white Storke, and also of the black Storke, called
(Saracinessa) is evil of nourishment, and hard to be endewed, and stinking.

The flesh of the Bitter, and Sea Coote, is good and sound, especially when
the Sea Coote does feed and scour her with sand, it is good to enseame and
breath a hawke: although naturally all water fowls are cold of complexion,
moist and hard of disgestion.

The flesh of the white Heron, otherwise called the Shoueler, and of the blew
or ash colored Heron, is of good disgestion and nourishes well.

The flesh of Finches, hedge Sparrowes, and such like, are in manner all one:
and you must beware that you give not your hawke too much of them at once.

The Cuckoes flesh is indifferent good for a hawke.

The flesh of a Hearon royal, that is to say the gray Hearon, is reasonable
good, according to the opinion of many ancient Falconers: but Ptholomeus king
of Egypt does allege the contrary, saying, that naturally all fowls which
live upon fish, frogs, or snakes, and such like venomous worms, are evil of
disgestion, and that their blood breeds opilations and evil humors,
although they seem to delight hawks to feed on them for their delicate
sweetness and taste.

The flesh of the red Haro^ is good: but you must give but little of it.

The flesh of the Swan, and many other kinds of water fowls to long here to
be rehearsed, are to be used according to the time and circumstances of
occasion.

The flesh of Sea Crows, and Ravens, Cormorants, and such like, are of a
brackishe blood, and therefore you may now and then give your hawke a gorge
thereof: but you must not use it often.

The flesh of ravening birds, as Kightes, Puttocks, Harpies, Eagles, Eyrons,
and such like, all these are very contrary to the nature of Falcons, and are
stinking, of evil disgestion, and chollericke. Their blood and brains are
worst of all, for they breed perilous worms.

The flesh of all birds which feed upon seeds, as Feasants, Pertriche,
Quayles, and such like, are the best that can be, especially when a falcon or
any other Hawke is sick.

Doves flesh is excellent, either when a hawke is sick, or to mew withal: for
it makes a hawke to mewe well and quickly.

Pullets flesh, and Chickens flesh, is good at all times, and always in
season, as the flesh of that fair foul Bella Donna is.

To man and make a Hawke: and first of a Niasse Falcon.
depiction of Niasse falcon

THere are three sorts of Falcons. viz. Niasses, Sorehawkes, and Hawks, taken
praying for themselves at large, which our Falconers call ramage or slight
Falcons. And I am of opinion that they are all made after one sort, and are
manned much a like. But by mine advice no man shall much assure himself in a
Niasse, because over and besides that (for the most part) they prove not well,
they do also require great pains and attendance with extreme patience, to
make them kill and stoop a foul well, or to flee a high pitche. Nevertheless
if any man will needs be doing with them, he should first make a Niasse to
the Hearon from the fist, I mean both the blew and read Hearon, and to such
other great fowls. For as much as the Niasse hawks are naturally good
seazers, bold, and hardy birds. And after they be well in blood, and entered in
fleeing to those fowls from the fyst, then may you make them to the River,
going into some fair large field, whereas there may be either some wild
Pecockes, flesh Crows, or some other great foul, bearing your Falcon on your
fist: loose her hood in a readiness, drawing as near the foul as you can,
and the first foul or bird that springs, vnhoode her and let her flee from
the fyst to the same foul, because they may draw your Hawke vpwardes. And
when she is at a great gate, or at a reasonable pitche, then throw her out a
Ducke, or a Mallarde seeled with a feather through the nares. And if she kill
it, then reward her well, and feed her upon it with as much favor as you
can devise, always lewring and crying to her to comfort and encourage her.

Of Ramage Falcons.
IF a falconer chance to recover a Ramage Hawke which was never handled
before, let him immediately seele her, and therewithal let him even then also
put on her Iesses, the which must be of Shameuse leather, or soft Calves
leather, or such other leather as may be gentle and plyaunte to her leg.
At the end thereof it shall not be amiss to set two Veiuelles of Silver,
the one thereof may have the arms of the King, or Queen whom you serve, and
the other a Scutcheon of your own arms. For as much as when they flee out, if
they chance to be taken up, they may the sooner be returned again and
restored to their owners, the which must then remember to reward the taker up
of his Hawke liberally. You shall also put her on a a pair of good Belles with
two propre bewets: Being thus furnished, you shall go about to man her,
handling her often gently, and both to avoid the sharpness of her beak, as
also the better to rebuke her from biting and nipping: you shall have a
straight smooth stick, as big as your finger, and half a foot long or
more, with the which you shall gently stroke your hawke about the pinions of her
wings, and so downwardes thwart all her train. And if she chance to knappe or
byte at the stick, let her bite hardly, for that will rebuke her thereof,
whereas your hand being twitched away fearfully, would make her proceed the
more eagerly. To man her well you must watch all the night, and keep her on
your fiste: and you must teach her to feed seeled: and having a great and
easy rufterhoode, you must hoode and vnhoode her oftentimes seeled as she
is, handling her gently about the head, and coying her always when you
vnhoode her, to the end she take no disdain or displeasure against her
keeper. And also make her to plume and tire sometimes upon a wing, and keep
her so on the fist day and night without perching of her, until she be weary,
and suffer you to hoode her gentely and stir not: and correct her of her
ramage toys, especially of snapping and biting, stroking her evermore as
before said with your stick. But if it happen (as it does sometimes) that
your chance be to have a Falcon so ramage and shrewde metteld that she will
not leave her snapping and biting, then take a cloue of Garlike clean pilled,
or a little Aloes Cycatrina, and when she byteth or snappeth at your hand or
stick, offer her the Garlike or Aloes, and let her bite it: for either the
strong sent of the Garlike, or the bitter taste of the Aloes, will quickly
make her leave her biting and snapping.

To use a Hawke to the hoode.
IT happens oftentimes that Falconers have Hawks which come from Cyprus,
Candya, Alexandria, and other far countries, the which having been in the
hands of such as could not well skill of them, become coye and very
untoward to be hooded, and will hardly be woonne to abide the Hoode by any
means. In this case you must first seele your Hawke, and being seeled, you
must fit her with a large easy Hoode: and hoode and vnhoode her often
therewith, watching her a night or two, and handling her oftentimes about
the head as before said, until she have forgotten that fault. And when she
once does leave it, you may unseal her in an Evening by Candle light,
handling her still softly with your hand about the head, hooding and
vnhooding her oftentimes, until she will well abide the Hoode and brook to
be handled. And here I think good to express mine opinion, that he which
takes in hand to be a Falconer, ought first to be very pacient, and
therewythall to take syngular delyght in an Hawke, so that he may seem to be
in love (as it were naturally) with his Hawke, even that a man would say, it
were a thing bredde so in the bone as it could never be rooted out of the
flesh. For such a man with never so little pain and industrye, will become
an excellent Falconer: but he which takes not that delyght in his Hawke, but
does rather exercise it for a pomp and boast, than upon a natural instinct:
or being a poor manne, does use it to get his living, such a man in mine
opinion shall seldom prove a perfect Falconer, but a marrehawke, and shall
bear the bag after a right Falconer. To turn to my purpose, when your Hawke
being so seeled, does feed well, and will abide the Hoode, and to be
handled, without stryking or biting at your hand: then in an Evening by
Candlelyght you shall vnseele her, and with your finger and a little spyttle,
annoynte the place where the seeling threade was drawn thorough. And when you
have hooded her, take her on your fyste, and hold her so all night until
day appear again, doing off her Hoode oftentimes, and handling her gently
with your hand, strokyng her softly about the wyngs and the body, hooding
and vnhooding of her, and giving her sometimes to feed, a morsel or twain,
or sometimes tyring or plumage. But above all things, you must watch her on
the fyste, so many nights together, without setting her down on any
pearche, that she may be weary, and suffer you to hoode and handle her
gently without any manner of resistaunce, and until she have altogether left
and forgotten her stryking and biting at your hand: but some Hawks will be
long before they leave that fault, as the more coy or ramage that they be, the
longer they will retain those ill tatches, and will not peradventure be woonne
from them in three, four, or five days. When she is well reclaymed from it,
then you may let her sit upon a pearche to rest her. But every night you
shall do well to keep her on the fyste three or four hours, handling her,
and strokyng her gently, and causing her to tire or to plume, always making of
her, and hooding and vnhooding her oftentimes as before said. And the like
may you do also by day light, but in a Chamber a part, where she may see
no great light until she feed surely and eagerly without dread.

To make your Hawke know your voice.
IF your hawke be thus in four or five days manned, so that she begin to
feed eagerly and boldly, then you shall first begin to make her know
your whistle, or the chirping of your mouth: and afterward your voice, in
this manner. Take a quick Pullette, and going into some secret place, where
your Hawke may well perceive the foul, and yet see no great open light, let
her plume and feed upon it, as she sits upon your fyst: then chyrke with
your voice, and use those other soundes which Falconers do to their Hawks: and
feed her so hooding her gently: then afterward you may let her plume a little
upon some wing, being still hooded, as well to loose her in the head, and to
make her cast water, as also to teach her the better how to sit on the
fyste. The feeding for a Falcon shall be Pullettes, not very old, Calves
hearts, wethers hearts, and hogs hearts, and to give her a convenient gorge,
to the end she may the better digest both the gross substance, and the
slimie matter. But if your Falcon be not eager or sharp set, then shall you do
well to wash her meat sometimes in fair water, and some other whiles in
urine, wringing it a little and then feeding her therewith, for one, two, or
three gorges, and that not continually: but respecting a day or two between,
and that is referred to the discretion of the good Falconer. For this is done
somewhat to abate a Hawke and to enseame her. It shall not be amiss also in
the morning when she is empty both in the gorge and panel, to convey into her
a little Sugercandie, to the quantity of a small nutte, for that dissoluing in
her, will make her the better to endure, and will both break the gross
substance, and digest the glitte in her, and also will make her eager, as
shall be further said hereafter.

How to make a hawke know her feeding.
WHen your Hawke feeds eagerly, and knows your whistle and your voice, then
may you teach her to know her feeding, and to bate at it in this wise. You
should with your right hand show her meat, crying and lewring to her aloude,
and if she bate or strike at it, then must you quickly and handsomely let her
foot it and feed on it for three or four bittes: and do thus oftentimes, to
the end she may the better know her feeding. And afterward feed her, and
give her every night (without intermission) some casting either of feathers,
or of cotton, with two Cloves, sometimes cut in four pieces, and put into the
casting, or a little Aloes wrapped up in the Cotton, according as the Falconer
shall see that it is requisite. For such castings make a Hawke clean and
eager. ()

To make a Falcon bold and ventrous.
depiction of falcon

WHen a Falcon has learned to feed, and to know the call of her keeper: then
to make her hardy, you shall suffer her to plume a pullet or good great chicke^.
And you shall go into some close place, where she may not see overmuch light, as
before said, where loosing her hoode in a readiness, you must have a live
pullet in your hand, and kneling on the ground, lewring and crying alowde unto
her, make her plume and pull the pullet a little. Then with your teeth drawing the
strings, vnhoode her softly, suffering her to plume and pluck it with her beak
twice or thrice more, cast out the pullet upon the ground before her. Then
must you with raising or holding down your fiste, encourage her until she
leap down upon the pullet, and seize it. Then when she begins to break it
and to take blood, you shall lewre and cry alowde unto her, and encourage her
by all the means yt you can, feding her upon the ground. And therwithal you
shall take her up gently and nymbly with the pullet in her foot, whereon let
her plume, and feed now and then a little. Then hoode her gently, and at
last give her tyring of a wing or a foot of the said pullet.

To make a Falcon know the lewre.
AFter that your Falcon has twice or thrice thus killed a pullet in some
secret place, the^ must you make her know ye lewre in this wise. Fasten a
pullet unto your lewre, and go a part. Then give your Falcon to hold unto some
other man, who may draw loose ye strings of her hoode in a readiness. And when
you are gone a little back from him, take your lewre at half ye length of ye
string, and cast it about your head once or twice, lewring with your voice
also. Then let yt other vnhoode your hawke, whiles you throw out ye lewre not
far from your hawke, lewring and crying still unto her. And if your hawke do
stoop to ye lewre, and seize ye pullet, suffer her to plume her, coying her,
and lewring still with your voice. Then let her feed on the pullet upon the
lewre, and afterward take her upon your fiste together with her meat, and
hoode her, suffering her to plume and tire, as is before said.

How to call your Falcon loose, and at large.
WHen your Falcon has come well three or four times unto the lewre in some
secret place, as well to a live pullet as to a dead, then shall you go
abroad into some fair meadow, where are no trees, and fastening a quick pullet
unto ye lewre, give your hawke to hold unto another man. The^ tying also a
cryance unto your hawks lease, cause that other which holds your hawke to
make ready her hoode, and give her a little bytte of meat on his fiste,
chirping and cheering her with his voice: Even therwithall do you go abacke four
or five paces or more, lewring twice or thrice, let him which holds the
hawke, do off her hoode, then do you take the lewre at length of the string,
and cast it about your head, crying and lewring alowde, throwing it upon ye
ground: and if your hawke stoop at the pullet, suffer her to break it, and
feed her upon ye lewre, casting her to eat the brains and the heart of the
pullet with the lewre also, always crying and lewring. And this order shall
you observe daily further and further off, until she be well lewred, entered,
and manned.

How to call a Falcon, that will come loose.
WHen your Falcon will come a far off unto the lewre, and stoop to it being
thrown out, without any coyness or ramagenesse, then setting her sharp, you
shall get on horseback in a morning, and go into some fair plain field, and
as near as you can, where there is no wood nor trees, there giving your hawke
unto some other man to hold (which must also be on horseback.) Put your
cryance to your hawke in such wise, that she may not tangle her self therewith
in coming to ye lewre. The^ drawing back a little as much as you think
meet, give a sign to him yt holdeeth ye hawke, to make ready her hoode, and
let him hold up his fist on height. Then lewre you three or four times as loud
as you ca^, always casting ye lewr about your head, whereunto for ye first time
I would have a Pullette fastened still. And while you so do, let him which
holds your hawke, pluck of her hoode, and if she come straight to the lewre,
forbear until she come within eight or ten paces of you, then cast it unto
her. And if she take the lewre, let her plume thereupon, and lewre you still
with your voice, lighting of your horse, and draw near to your hawke fair and
softely, lewring and crying unto her, and so feed her as before said. But after
that she be called two or three days to ye lewre on horseback with a cryance,
or more or less, according to ye towardness or untowardness of the hawke, if
she come roundely a bow shot from you, you may then go out in a morning,
having set her reasonably sharp for the purpose, and call her loose on
horseback, yt is without either lease or cryance, but loose, and in company.
And if she come to you, feed her upon the lewre, as beforesayde, lewring still
unto her, to make her acquainted with your voice. And the next day you may
call her to the dry lewre, without a Pullette, or any thing upon it. And when
she is come to the lewre, cast her out a quick Pullette, breaking first the
feet and legs thereof, and let her kill it upon the lewre, and feed her up.

To make a Falcon fleeing.
WHen your hawke will come, and stoop to the lewre roundly and without any
ramagenes, the^ if she be a haggard, you must put her on a pair of great
lewring bells, and the like shall you do also to a soarehawke. And so much the
greater ought your bells to be, by how much more you see your hawke
gyddyheaded, or like to rake out at cheek. For it ca^ be no hurt to clogge her
with great bells at ye first, until her conditions be known and well perceived.
That being done, and having also set her sharp, go one morning on horseback
into some fair large field, without wood or trees, if it be possible, and having
your hawke upon your fist, consider of ye wind, and ride you up into ye wind,
or toward that way as the wind blows, half a bow shot. And having
loosened your hawks hoode, whystle softely, as it were to provoke your hawke
to flee. Whereupon she will begin to bate, or at least to flappe with her
flagges and sails, and to advance her self upon your fist. Then suffer her
until she rowse or mewte, and when she has done either of them, vnhoode her,
and let her flee with her head into the wind. For thereby she shall be the
better able to get up on wing, and to get into the wind. Then will your Falcon
naturally clyme vpwardes, rouing and fleing round. Therefore when you see that
she has flowen two or three times, you shall cry and lewre with your voice,
and cast the lewre about your head, whereunto first tie a pullette, as before
said, and if your Falcon come in, when she approaches near you, the^ cast out
ye lewre into the wind, and if she stoop to it, reward her as before.

To make a Falcon leave the stand on the ground.
IF your Falcon at first when she flees from the fiste, will not get up, but
take stand on the ground, as the most part of soar Falcons do commonly, you
shall not yet therefore be discouraged, nor out of hope, but rather making
toward her with your horse, and threatening of her, seem to fear with your
wande, and drive her from the stand, until she flee a turn or two. Then
take her down to the lewre, and feed her. But if so chance yt your hawke
will not leave that fault of taking the stand, then must you seek to find
out some Choughe, Starling, or some such birds, making ready your hawks
hoode, draw as near them as you may, until they rise. Then vnhoode your
hawke, and no doubt if she will flee them, they will train her well upwards.
Then must you have in a readiness a Ducke seeled, as before said, and so as
she may not see but backewardes, because she may thereby mownte the higher
And holding her fast by one of the wings, near unto the body, in your right
hand, and lewring with your voice to make your Falcon turn the head,
forbear till she be at a reasonable pitche, then running under her, cast up
your Ducke toward her, that she may perceive it, and that the Ducke may be to
her in steed of a train. And if she strike her, or stoop her, or trusse
her, then suffer her to kill it, and reward her upon it, taking out one of her
legs, or both, if need require, feed her up with a reasonable gorge. This
being done once or twice, or oftener, according to the occasion, your hawke
will leave the stand, and delight to keep her on wing, and become also the
more obedient and loving.

To make the Falcon to the River.
WHen your Falcon is accustomed to flee for it, and will lie upon you at a
great gate, or at a reasonable pitche, and will come and hold in the head at
your voice and lewring, then may you go to the River, where you shall find any
foul, and there shall it behooue you to use such policy, that you may cover
the foul, and get your hawke to a good gate, above the foul. And when her
head is in, then lay out the foul, and cry, Hey gar, gar, gar. And if your
Falcon do stoop them, and enewe them once or twice, then quickly thrust your
hand in your hawking bag, and make her a train with a Ducke seeled. And if
your hawke do either trusse or stoop it, succoure your hawke immediately,
crossing the Duckes wings, and let your hawke plume. etc. I am of opinion,
that for the first or second time that you show your hawke a foul, it shall
not be best to show her great foul, but rather small foul, as the
Dapchicke, or such like. For if you show them the greatest fowls at the
first, it often happens that they slyppe from the hawke up the wind, and
the hawke cannot recover them, (but raketh out after,) whereby the Falconer is
feign to trotte farther than he would, yea and sometimes also he leeseth his
hawke. Wherefore in my judgment it were better at the first to be merry and
wise.

To make a hawke inward at the River, when she raketh out.
IF it chance that your hawke rake out with a foul, and cannot recover it,
and in the end gives it over, and comes in again directly upon the man, then
shall you do well to cast her out a seeled Ducke. And if she stoop it, or
trusse it, cross the wings, and suffer her to take her pleasure, rewarding her,
and giving her the heart, brains, tongue, and liver, with a leg or two,
according as you see occasion. And for default of a quick Ducke, take her
down to the dry lewre, and let her plume a pullette, and feed her upon it.
Thus doing, your hawke will learn to give over a foul that rakes out, and
hearing the keeper lewre, she will learn the better to hold in the head,
and to make back again to the River.

To make a flight for a Haggarde.
HE that would make a flight for a Haggarde, he shall do well in my conceyte,
to flee where there be no Crows, Choughes, or such like, for the first two or
three flights, because she shall take none occasion to rake out after such
cheek. And it shall be good also, that you let her not flee out on head too far
at the first, but run after her, and cry to her ( VVhyloe, whyloe) make her
turn head. And when she is come in, take her down with the lewre, unto the
which I would have a quick pullette fastened, as before said. And you shall
suffer her to tire, plume, and feed as before, etc. It happens oftentimes,
yt a Haggard through her gadding moore and gallantnesse of mind, does ra^gle
out from her keeper, more tha^ upon any other cause. Then shall you clogge her
with greater lewring bells, and make her a train or two with a ducke seeled, to
teach her hold in, and know the man. You shall not need to train a Haggard
so often as a Soarehawke, because they have been accustomed to pray for
themselves, and do by experience know one foul from an other. But they do
much better remember either the rebukes, or cherishings which are made to
them, than Soarehawkes do: and therefore it behooves to use more care and heed
about a Haggarde, than a soar Falcon, and above all things to take her often
down with the dry lewre, and to let her take her pleasure of her reward.
And (as Falconers term it) to be ever well in blood. For otherwise she will
not long be at your commandment, but make you follow her. ()

How to make your Falcon kill her foul at the first.
depiction of falcon

WHen a Soar Faulcon, or a Haggard is well lewred, and flies a good gate, or
a reasonable pitch, and stoupeth well, then shall you first cast off a well
quarried or make Hawke, and let her stoop a foul upon a brook or a plashe,
and watch her until she put it to the plunge: then take down your make Hawke,
and reward her, and hood her up, setting her a little way off by the flight,
that you may use her help afterward if need be. This being done, take your
young hawke which is not yet entered, and going up the wind half a boweshotte,
or thereabouts, loose her hood, and softly whistle her off the fiste, until
she have rowsed or mewted, then let her flee with her head into the wind,
having first given fhew to your company that they be in a readiness against
the Hawke be at a good gate, and to show water, and lay out the foul. This
order being observed, and running and crying as Falconers use to do, hawke on
your Faulcon, and give her leave to get up, and when she is at a reasonable
pitch, and covering the foul, give a sign to your companions that they draw
near to the water, and all at once make in upon the foul on all sides the
brook (as Falconers term it) to land her: then if your Falcon stoop and
strike or trusse it, run in apace to help her, and crossing the fowls wings,
let your Hawke take her pleasure on it, feeding her as custom is: but if so
chance that she cannot slay it at the first stouping, then you must give
your Hawke respite and time to recover her gate: And when she is at her gate
again, and her head in, then lay out the foul again as before said, until
you may land it at the last, always remembering that as soon as she seize it,
you succor her quickly and reward her according to order. True it is that to
be assured, you shall do well always to have a quick Mallard or Ducke in the
Hawking bag whenever you would make a flight: and if your Hawke kill not
the foul which is stouped (as often happens by many overthwart cha^ces) then
may you quickly have recourse to the hawking bag, and feeling your live Ducke
(your Hawke being at her pitche and her head in) you may throw her up to your
Hawke, and reward her etc. For this order shall alwais maintain your hawke to be
inwards, and in good life, and blood.

How to do when your river Hawke will take stand in a tree.
IF you have a Falcon which (as soon as she has once or twice stouped and
endewed a foul) will take stand on a tree, you must as much as may be, eschew
