her nares, and therefore see that ye guard her eye with a wet cloute to keep it
from the smoke. All such manner of fires must be given toward the evening,
before Hawks are supped, when they are empty, for otherwise ye handling of
them would make them cast their gorges. When all is done as it should be, half
gorge your Hawke, or somewhat less with warm meat. And the same day make
provision of such Snayles as are among vines, or among Fenill, and such as
have gray shelles, they are the best, for men are wont to eat of them. Steep
five or six of them in the milk of an Ass, or of a Got, or else (for want
of that) in woman's milk, and let it be done in a good large glass well
covered, that they creep not out.

The next morning break the shelles, and wash them in new milk as it comes
from the Cow: then give four or five of those Snayles to your Hawke, after
that they be of bigness. And as soon as that is done, set her against a fire
or the Sun, and remove her not away till she have muted four or five times.
And if she can abide the heat well, let her alone still, for it does her much
good. After noone feed her with a Hens leg or with some small birds, or with
a Ratte or a Mouse, which are best of all, and then set her in a warm place
and give her not too great a gorge. When evening commes, that she has endued
and put over her meat, take five or six Cloves of Mace, broken asunder, and
wrapped in a piece of flesh or a pellet of cotton, and make her to receive it
by fair means, or foul, by opening her beak, and conueying it into her.
Continue this medicine four or five days, and your Hawke shall recover.
Afterward make her to tire evening and morning and let her feeding be styped in
milk as is said before of the Snayles, for the milk scowreth her body within,
and is very nutritive, and will quickly bring her to be hie, and in flesh
again.

Another medicine that Mallopin gives in stead of the other aforesaid.
TAke the powlder of Saffron and Camomill, of each the mountenance of a little
peaze, and when ye have mingled the^ together, put thereto Larde that is
neither restie, nor over salted, and steep them a night and a day in three or
four changes of water, and then wash the Larde thoroughly in fair licoure.
That done, take Sugar clarified, and the Maree of a Beef. Of the things
aforesaid, take as much of the one, as of the other, so as you may make five
or six balls of the bigness of a Bean. Then myngle the said mixtures, and
the powders together, and every morning give your hawke one of the balls, till
all be spente: and as is said before, set your hawke by the fire, or in the
Sun, and feed her not by the space of an hour or twain after: at which
time you shall give her either a Hens leg, or some small birds, or a Rat,
or some Myce. And in the morning, when she has well indewed, give her four or
five cloves of Maces lapped in a little flesh, or in the skin of a Hen, or
in pellettes of Cotton. And so may you cauterize her before the said medicin,
after the manner that I have showed before in the former receyte of the Snayles,
so you draw her meat in Milk, or in fresh butter.

For the disease of the ears, which comes of the Rhewme and cold.
SOmetimes there happens another disease to hawks, by reason of moisture of
the head, which is called the disease of the ears, because there issue out
certain humors by them. And ye shall know the disease by this, that the hawke
will often times wrythe her head back, and makes not so good cheer, as she
should do, and is more unlusty. Wherefore search and peruse her ears, and
you shall find the disease there. The remedy whereof, by Master Ame Cassians
devise, is this.

Take a little long iron, round at the end as a peaze, and Oil of sweet
Almonds, or for lack of that, Oil of Roses, which is much better, if you
can come by it. Then heat your iron in the fire, neither glowing red, nor very
hot, and put it into the Oil, and of that oil so heat with the iron, drop
a little into your hawks ears, putting the iron a little into them, that they
be not stopped. For then of such inconvenience happens oftentimes the Canker
to the brain, which is incurable, and kills the hawke. And beware of
thrusting the iron to far in, or of being too hot, for else you may kill
her. You must continue the ministering of this Oil, four or five days,
always wiping away the humors gently, that yssewe out of her ears, and
always respecting her casting, whither it be clean or no. And if you list to
skowre her with a common pyll or twain, they will ease her head maruelously
well, and do her exceeding much good: or if you do it with the said balls
of Larde, Sugar, and Marow of Beef, it is good likewise, for you may use
either ye one or ye other at your pleasure.

Of the disease of the eylyddes, which comes of the rewme and cold.
ANother disease happens to Hawks, in the eyelyddes, which causes a
swelling under the the eylydde, between the eye and the fear of ye beak (we
have no proper speech for it) if ye remedy it not betymes, it will swell
round about: and thereof comes the Hawe in the eye, which will ouergrow the
eye, and stop it. And assure your self it is a sign of death, if it grow
too long. For I have seen many dye of it in my time, for lack of remedy. Now
by Master Ame Cassyans opinion, the remedy is this. Heat the little round
iron that I spoke of before, and cauterize her with it softly upon her head, as
is said for ye Rewme. Likewise with ye other cutting iron sear her between
the eye and the beak. Also pierce her nares with the little iron, and
afterward give her the medicine of the Snayles, after the manner aforesaid,
four or five days together. And for want of that medicine, you may use the
other, of Larde, Sugar, and the Marow of Beef mingled with the powder of
Saffron and Camomill.

Of the Hawe in the eye, which comes of moisture, and cold, and how it
happens.
Moreover sometimes there grows a great disease in their eyes, which is named
the Hawe, and comes after the same manner that it comes in horses: namely,
sometime by a blow, or a stripe, sometime by a disease in the head, and most
commonly by hurting of the eye with the streyghtnesse of the hoode, or by some
other misfortune, which cannot sometimes be eschewed. And you shall discern
the coming of this disease, by seeing a little filme growing up from the
bending of her beak, and covering her eye by little and little. And this filme
is somewhat black before, and is called the Hawe, which puts out the eye, if
it once ouergrowe the ball of it. To remedy the same, take a little needle yt
is very sharp poynted, and fine threeded with a silk threede, and therewith
take up the Hawe handsomely, and cut it with a little slicer, as horse-leaches
do too horses, but beware that you cut it not too much, for hurting of the
eye, which you must wash with Rose water three days together. In these cures
of diseases that grow in the eyes, there must be great care used, for fear of
a greater mischief, because of the dayntinesse of the place.

Of a blow given to the eye, or of some other mischaunce.
SOmetime the eyes of hawks are hurt by some mishappe, some stripe, or
otherwise, as I said before. Against such unlooked for mischaunces, Master Ame
Cassyan gives clear Fenell water, and Rose water, as much of the one as of the
other, and therewith washes ye eye twyce or thrice aday. Master Malopin in his
book of the Prince, Arondell in French, is Hirundo, a Swallowe, otherwise
called Chelidon.
wills to take the juice of Celondine, otherwise called, Herb Arondell, or
Swallows herb, and to convey it into the eye. And if it be not to be had
green, to take it dry, and to beat it into powder, and to blow it into her
eye with a quill, and this shall recure the hawke.

Of the Filme in the eye, which some call the Veroll, or the Pin and web.
THere is another disease in the eye, called a Filme, which comes sometimes
of disease in the head, and of Rhewmes that distill into the eyes, and
sometimes of standing too long, or too close hooded, which happens through the
fault and negligence of such as have the bearing and oversight of them. For
the remedy heerof, Master Martin says, that ye must take Celondine, and
bray it, putting thereto Hoonie and fresh butter, and of each of those three
give your hawke a like portion with a hot gorge, and moreover put the powder
of Pepper and Aloes in her eye. Or else (as says Master Ame Cassian) you
must give her the foresaid medicine of Larde, Sugar, and Marow of Beef,
three or four days together, to skowre her, setting her by a fire, or in the
Sun, and feeding her after it with some live foul, and keeping her out of
the wind, and from standing cold, or moist. After she is so skowred, if ye
see that the web show it self much, cauterize her upon the upper part of
her head, and likewise a little between the eye and the beak, after the manner
aforesaid. When all this is done, squirte a little Rose water into her eye,
and if need be, minister thereto the powder or the juice of Celondine,
otherwise called Herb Arondell, as is said before. This disease of the Pin
and web, is of some men called the Veroll, for the remedying whereof, they
burn the shell of a Tortoyse in a new pot, and beat it into fine powder,
which they serce through a fine cloth. Then take they a Cockle of the sea,
which is fashioned like a Heart, and burning it thoroughly in the fire, make it
into fine powder, and serce it likewise. And finally they take Sugar Candy in
powder. These three powders myxte together in equal portions, they use to put
into their hawks eyes till they be whole.

Master Michelin tells of one other receyte for the said disease, which is
this. Make a little hole in the top of an egg, and power out the white of it:
then coil clear Rose water, and Sanguis draconis, well together, and fill up
your egg with them, and stir them thoroughly with a small stick. Afterward
wrappe up your egg in paste, and stop up the hole of it, that nothing get
out: which done, set it so clozed in the fire, till the paste become black and
read at the taking it from the fire. Then take out that which is within it,
and beat it into powder, and serce it through a fine cloth: and of that powder
you may use to put in your hawks eye till it be cured, washing her eye now
and then with water of Fenell, and of Roses.

Master Mallopin makes another medicine for the same disease, which is this.
Take the dung of a Lyzart, (which is called a Provincial) and beat it into
powder, with Sugar Ca^die, somewhat more in quantity than the other, mingling
the^ both together. He says, that this powder is much better than all the
others, whereof you may use as is said before, conueying into your hawks eye
water of Roses, and of Fenell.

For the disease that breeds in hawks beakes, commonly called Formica.
Diverse times there grows a disease upon the horn of hawks beakes, which
eats and fretteth the beak from the head. Master Ame says, it is a woorme
that eats the horn of the beak within, by reason whereof the Hawke is in
great danger, if she be not helped in time. Ye shall perceive it by this,
that the horn of the beak waxes rugged, and the beak begins to ryue and
cliue from her head. Master Ame Cassian gives this answer and remedy
thereunto. Take the gall of an Ore, (or of a Bull, which is better than of an
Ox) and alltoo beat it, and break it in a dishe, and put thereto the powder
of Aloes Cicotrine, and myngle them well together. Then noynte the horn of
your hawks clappe or beak therewith, and the very place where the Formica
grows, twyce adaye. But beware that you touch neither her eyes, nor her
nares. And continewe your so doing, till she be thoroughly cured, and let her
be bathed with Orpiment and Pepper, to keep her from vermin and Mytes.

For the disease that breeds in the Nares of Hawks.
ANother disease bredeth in Hawks nares, so as they swell exceedingly: and
sometime upon the horn of the beak there ryseth a cruste, at ye removing
whereof the flesh is found to be raw underneath the clappe, insomuche that
diverse times they loose ye one half of their beak. Master Ame Cassian says,
yt the hawke has small Mites in her head, which creep down alo^gst her
beak, and entering in at her nares, do bread ye said disease: and that ye hawke
feeling them, and being molested therewith, thrusts her talants into her
nares. Or else it happens sometimes, that a cast of hawks do buckle and crab
together, and thereof breeds the said disease. Master Ame Cassian provides
for it this remedy following. Here is left out the manner of cauterizing a
hawks nares* because the Italian has set it down.
Make little matches of paper, of the bygnesse of the tag of a point, and let
your hawke be cast handsomely, and set your matches on fire with a candle, and
sear your hawke upon the place swollen, taking good head that you do it not
too roughly. Which being done, annoint it the next morning with a little Hens
grease, and so will it heal well, and her beak and nares will not be stuffed,
but remain open. Nevertheless ye must be feign sometimes to touch her with
an iron, which is more dangerous than the other.

The disease called the Frownce, which breeds within hawks beakes, and in
their tongues.
THe Frownce proceeds of moist and cold humors, which descend from the
hawks head to their palate, and ye root of ye tongue. And of yt cold, is
engendered in ye tongue ye Frownce, otherwise called (of the French me^ ye
Barbillons, or Sourchelons.) by means of which they loose their appetite, and
cannot close their clap, whereof they oftentimes dye: and that disease is named
ye Eagles bane. For as I reported to you in ye first part of this collection,
the Eagle seldom when dies of age, but only by mean her beak does
ouergrowe, so as she cannot feed and gorge her self. Ye may perceive this
disease by loss of her appetite to feed. And to know it the better, open your
hawks beak, and look on her tongue, whither it be swollen or no: And if
there appear not that disease, open her beak again within a while after, and
see if there be any likelyhoode of it, and so may ye easily discrie the
mischief. For remedy whereof the said Master Mallopin says, that you must
take Oil of sweet Almondes, or Oil Olyue washed in four or five waters, and
with that Oil annoint her throat and her tongue three or four times a day with
a feather, for five or six days together. And if your hawke cannot feed,
let her meat be cut and shredde into very small pellets. This done, open
her beak gently, and make her to receive it down, by conueying a small stycke
into her throat, gyuing her not paste half a gorge at a time, and that must
be either of Mutton, or of some live foul, Hen, Chicken, or such like:
five or six days after, open her beak handsomely again, and with a pair of
sharp Sissers, cut off the typpes of the Barbillons, till the blood
follow, but yet beware of cutting away too much. After this, annoynte and
moysten well her throat with syrope of Mulberies, called of the Apothecaries
Diamoron, and then annoynte her with Oil of sweet Almondes, or with Oil
Olyue, till she be recured.

Of the disease called by the French men, Escorchillons, a kind of frownce or
Canker.
Escorcer in French, is to rippe off the rind or skin of any thing, of which
word this disease seems to be deryued.
SOmetimes there happens a disease to hawks, which is called the
Escorchillons, a hard disease to be discerned. It breeds commonly of a
Rhewme, confirmed in the head, from whence spring many other diseases,
(whereof I have made mention in the Chapter of Rhewmes in the head, and of the
disease called (the Barbillons,) which breeds in Hawks tongues,) as the
Pyppe does, the disease of the palate, and the Canker, which are very
dangerous diseases and deadely. If ye will know the Escorchillons, let your
hawke be cast handsomely, and open her beak, and force down her tongue with
your fingers end, so as you may see her windepype, and a little beneath her
windepype ye shall find the Escorchillons, like three or four sharp pricks
growing one against another, that sometimes the Hawke cannot cast, by mean
thereof. And that is a perfect way to know this evil. Furthermore, in the
same place, and on either side of the windepype, ye shall find two small
stertes of deshe, which are natural to all Hawks. But at the lower end of
them do grow up many little pricks, which are the cause that a Hawke can
not well cast in the morning, insomuche that sometimes she is feign to cast
her casting by peecemele, and not whole. And that is another assurance of the
said disease, which may be well cured and remedied both together.

The remedy which Ame Cassyan gives for this disease, is set forth in the
former Chapter by Master Mallopin, where he wylleth you to take Oil of sweet
Almondes, or Oil Olyue, etc.

The disease of the Canker which breeds in the throtes and tungs of Hawks.
YE must understand that the Canker breeds of foul feeding your Hawks, not
washing of their meat in cold water in Summer, and in warm in Winter, which
ingendreth in their guts gross slimy matter. And when those humors come to
be moved, they fume up into the head, and (so distilling again) engender heat
of the liver, which breaks out in the throat and the tung, and there engender
the Canker. You shall discern this disease by the feeding of your Hawke, for
in taking her meat she lets it fall, and afterward has much ado to
swallowe it. Therefore let her beak be anointed, and you shall find the
disease of the Canker. Master Ame Cassian gives this medicine for it. Take
Oil of Almonds or Oil Olife washed as is said before, and anoint well her
throat with it twice or thrice a day. That done, give her the said medicine of
Sugre, Lard, and marrowe of Beef three days together, and feed her with
Mutton or with Pullets or Hens flesh dipped in the foresaid Oil, but ye must
not wash your Oil of Almonds. After this, you must behold and regard the
Canker, and if you find it white, take a small iron made at the one end like a
Rasor, and at the other end edged and sharp. And if her tung be very much
ouergrowen with the Canker, slit and open it handsomely along the side of her
tung, and with your Raser scrape away the whitenes softly which you see there.
Then take a little Cotton or lint to dry and drink up the blood of her tung,
and see that none be left. And if the other side of her tung happen to be so
too, slit it likewise: which done, take the juice of Maydenheare, and lay upon
it. And for want of that herb, take a little bineger, or rather the juice of a
Lymon which is much better, and wash her meat in Oil till she be thoroughly
recured. Master Michelin gives another remedy which is this. Anoint well her
throat and tung with Sirup of Mulberies (otherwise called Diamoron) two or
three days together, after which take of the foresaid good Oil: Then take
the powder of Brimston and of sugre candie, or of other white sugar mingled
well together of each a like, and put a little thereof upon the Canker: for it
you should lay much, it would fret the tung too sore. And this manner of dealyng
is better for a confirmed Canker, than any other. Therefore wash her meat with
the oil aforesaid, and feed her with mutton, or the flesh of Hens or
pullets.

Of a kind of Pippe that is in a Hawke.
THe Pippe comes chiefly of cold and moystnesse of the head: and sometimes of
feeding your hawke with evil and rotten flesh, without washing it and making
it clean in warm water in the winter, and in cold water in the Summer.
Whereof ingendereth slimy and gross humors in the body, which ascend up to
the head and engender the pip on the tip of the tung as ye see commonly fall
out in chickens. And ye may perceive this disease by your hawks often sniting,
and by making a noyze twice or thryce in her snyting. M. Ame Cassian says,
that to remedy this disease, you must cast your hawke gently, and look upon
the tip of her tung: and if ye find her to have the pippe, ye must scour her
with a pill made of Agarik and Ierapigra, given two or three days together
with her casting toward night, and that will rid her of the rhewme in her
head, the rather if she be made to tire against the sun in the mornings, as
is said before. M. Malopin in his book of the Prince, says, that to cure the
Pippe, ye must bind a little cotton upon a sticks end, and dipping it in
sweet rosewater wash well her tung with it: and afterward annoint it three
or four days with oil of Almonds, and oil olife well washed as is before
said: and when ye have done so, ye shall find the Pippe all white and soft.
Then take an awle, and with the point of it lift up the Pippe softly, remouyng
it as women do Pippe their Chickens. Howbeit ye must not remove it till it be
full ripe: for if ye take it to green, you shall hurt your hawke. And look
that you wet her tung and palate twyce or thryce a day with the foresaid oil,
till she be thoroughly cured.

Of the disease of their palate which falls of swelling by reason of moisture
of the head.
SOmetimes it happens that the palates or roofes of hawks mouths are
swollen, and look whytish: which comes of moisture and cold wherewith their
heads are surcharged. And ye may perceive this disease by that they cannot
close their beakes, and by that they look not so cherely as they were wont to
do, ne can put over, or endew their meat but with great pain. To cure your
hawke of this disease, you must open her beak, where you shall find the
roof of her mouth whytish and swollen. And if you find it not so, you must
search her beak to discern if she have any other disease there that lettes
her to shet it: for sometimes their beakes grow more on the one side of the
clappe than on the other, so as they cannot close the^. The remedy that M. Ame
Cassian gives for this evil, is this: The hawks that are so diseased must
have the said pills of Larde, sugar, and maree of beef, given them every
morning, one or two, for four or five days space together, and about an
hour or two after, feed them with some poultrie or mutton drawn through the
foresaid oil. And after those days, open her beak again and softly scrape
of the whytenesse: and if ye find the swellyng abated, then do none other
thing to her but only continue your anointing of her with the said oil. But
if ye perceive the swellyng to ryze to high, ye may lance or prick it, but ye
must beware that ye stryke not to deep, for ye may soon kill your hawke.
Afterward lay the juice of maydenheare to it, and continue it till it be
thoroughly cured, and always draw her meat in the said oil, or else in milk
or butter.

For the disease of the jaws.
THe disease of the jaws comes either of drawing the hoode to streyt, or for
that it is to close and streyt of it self. And yt causes the Rewme to drop
down out of the hawks head upon her gummes and jaws, (if we may so term
them.) You shall know it by this, that she can neither open nor shet her beak.

M. Michelins medicine for the same.
Annoint well the gorge, jaws, and nares of your hawke with oil of sweet
Almondes three or four days together, and for want of that, take oil Olife
washed in two or three waters, and draw her meat through it as is said before,
and give her pills of Lard, sugre and marie of a beef, or else common pills
to scour her both upward and downward.

Of the Hawke that has broken her clappe, by some mischaunce.
AN other inconvenience befalls Hawks by the negligence of such as keep
them: for in their feeding there cleaneth or remains some piece of flesh in
their jaws or in the roof of their mouth, or on some place or other of their
beak, which marreth their beakes, so as it is enforced to fall away in
sliuers and pieces. This happens for want of wiping their beakes as they ought
to be after their feeding, by mean whereof both her clappes grow so much, as
at length it falls to breaking and riuing, if it be not remedied in time. And
thereof breeds this disease which we call ( Formica Corrosiua) whereby the
beak becomes brittle and is utterly marred. Master Ame Cassian apointeth it
this remedy following. Look into your Hawks beak, coping it and keeping it
very clean, and if you find any Formica corrosiua there, remove it. That done,
anoint the horn of her beak with the blood of a Snake or an Adder, and the
blood of a Hen, mingled together, to make it to grow the more speedyly. Also
let the meat which she eats be cut in small pellets, for otherwise she
cannot feed. And yet for all that, cease not to flee with her. Within.xv.
days or three weeks after, when ye see her beak begin to grow again, cast
your Hawke handsomely, and cope her nether clappe that the upper clappe may
join orderly unto it, as it should do of his own nature.

Of the falling sickness, which happens to hawks as well as to men, and
other living things.
THe chief Falconers say, that the falling sickness happens too hawks,
through a fumyng heat that ascends up from the liver to their heads, and
makes them to fall down upon the suddayne. M. Mallopin says, that to remedy
this disease, the hinder part of their head must be perused and sought, where a
man shall find two little pittes, which must be cauterized with a wire of
brass. And if that help not, then must you cauterize her dayntely upon the
head with the foresaid round iron, or else you may hap to kill her. This done,
dry read Lentils in an oven, and make them into fine powder: then take the
filyng of iron, the finest of it, as much of the one as of the other, and
mingle the^ both together with honey, and make it in little balls of the
bigness of a peaze. Then give your hawke two or three of them, puttyng them as
far into her gorge as you can, and hold her upon your fist at the fire or in
the sun til she have made a mute or twain, and let her have no meat till
noone, and then serve her of a Pigeons wing, dealing so with her seven or eight
days together: In the night let her be kept always abroad, and in the day
times in the dark, with water continually before her.

M. Ame Cassyan teaches another medicine: that is to wit, that the skin of
their heads must be launced right over against the foresaid pittes, where
there are little veins which must be taken up with a silk threede, and
anointed over with the blood of a Chicken. Which being done, ye must give her
the foresaid pills seven or eight days together, taking good heed that ye
set her not near any other hawks, and that your hawking glove be very clean.
For that kind of diseaze is contagious, and will soon pass from one hawke to
another, by feeding on the glove whereon another has been fed before. And by
night let her stand in the wind and open air, and by day in dark places,
with water always before her, as is already taught you.

Of another falling evil which first breeds in the neck and in the gorge of
a Hawke.
IF you perceive your hawke to have a swollen neck and gorge, and that she
pants more strongly in the mornings at one time than at another: assure your
self that she has the falling evil. Martin says, that you must take
Sanguis draconis, Nutmegs, that kind of Mirabolans, which are called
Kebuline, Cloves, Cynamon and Ginger, of each two penny weight, and making it
all into fine powder, strew a quantity of it every morning upon her meat,
supping her every night with a ratte or a mouse, three or four days together,
and that will make her whole and sound.

Of the Fistula, a grief that proceeds through pain of the head.
YE shall perceive when your hawke has ye Fistula, by the running of her nares
and by the streaming down of ye humors from her head. For yt which disease,
Martin allows this remedy. Ye must cast your hawke handsomely, and deplume
her head behind in the backer part, and anoint it with butter and swynes
blood together. And you shall find a vain that comes down to her eyes,
which you must cut, and knit it again with a red silk threede, anointing
it well and thoroughly with butter and swynes blood for nine days together, and
then it will recover her.

For the swymming in the head of a Hawke.
IF your hawke gape much and beat her wings, then be ye sure that she has ye
swymming in ye head. The remedy whereof is this. Take a fine needle that is
sharp poynted, and when ye have well het it in the fire, pearce her nares with
it through on both sides, and beware that ye go not awrie, for so ye may do
her great harm. Then anoint it with Oil and butter together, and it will
recover her, by mean of the vente that you shall give the humor by the nares.

For all manner of diseases in the head, and specially for the ache that is in a
Hawks head.
Whenever your hawke has any great disease or pain in her head, take six
graynes of pepper, four of Stauesaker, and five cloves, and beat them
together into sine powder, and feed her but three days together, with warm
meat mingled with it, and she shall recover. And for want of that, ye may use
the fine powder that is me^cioned heretofore. And if your hawke will not be
fed with it, let it be conveyed into cotton or into a a Hens skin, to take
away the sent of it, and feed her with none other than warm meat, and such
as is light of disgestion. For the diseases of the head, do so weaken her
appetite and stomach, that she cannot put over, nor indew her meat. And to
the end she may the better indew it, give her but small meals till she be
thoroughly recovered. And if she will eat the yolke of an egg, drop upon it
some of the said powder, and give it her with whote meat, and so ye shall
recover her. Here are sundry receytes and medicines, which I never have
proved, and therefore I can warrant little of them: but nevertheless I find
them in my French Authors, and therefore am so ventrous to place them here in
this collection of remedies for hawks: leaving them over to the desirous
Falconer, that has a will to practice upon his hawke. For store (they say) is
no sore, and among many there must needs fall out some good and wholesome
receytes. Wherefore judge discretely of all: and make proof of such as you
like: Experience is the mother of skill.

Of the stone, and how and whereof it comes.
YOu must understand yt there are.ij. sorts of diseases in hawks, called by the
name of the stone: and scarsly does the one come without the other. The one
keepth beneath in their tuels, and the other in their bowels and panels: and
they may be cured both together. Some call this disease ye Cray. And M. Ame
Cassian says that the stone or Cray comes by the eating of filthy flesh,
and by mean of foul feeding. For it burns and dries in their bowels, (as I
said heretofore in the diseases of the head) because the filthe which they have
gathered in their panels inflameth their Liver, which does so dry up the
substance of the guts, that they cannot mute, but must needs die of it, if
they be not cured. Some say that this disease comes of giving them washed meat
whote before it be thoroughly cold, and that is like enough: for a hawke likes
not of water and blood both together at once. The stone in the fundement comes
of the filthe which the hawke should mute, which thickeneth and lies bakte at
the tuell: by means whereof she becomes so poor, that she cannot mute or slyse
from her, and so must needs die. Yet notwithstanding I have often seen, that
when a Falcon is high and lusty, she will slyse it out well enough by mean of
her strength. And ye may perceive when she has the stone, by that she muteth
with pain and by drops, which is a sign that she needs to skowre that
matter, whereof the stone does grow. And when she muteth at twice, and a third
time after that, it is a token that the stone is thoroughly confirmed in her
guts and panel. Moreover, when ye see that her tuell is chased, and but
little drops from her, and that the feathers of her train are much filed
with her muting, and that she is evermore picking with her beak about her
tuell, be ye sure she has ye stone in her tuell, which we call the stone Craye.
Again when she muteth and makes as though she would iouke upon your fist, and
in her eyes is more troubled tha^ of ordinary: doubt not but that she has the
stone cray. And because she cannot rid it, she is in danger, if she be not
lookte too in time. The remedy thereof by the judgment of M. Ame Cassyan, is
this: take a slyce of Larde (or a pellet of Soap, wet in salet oil) of the
bigness of a goose quill, and an inche long, and put thereon the powder of
Aloes cicotrine: which done, cast your Hawke handsomely, and convey it into her
tuell as ye would give a man a suppositorie: and if the Larde be too tender
and soft to handle, stick it upon a Hens feather, so as the feather appear
not through the Larde (for so may ye do her great harm with the feather) and
so convey it up into her tuell, drawyng away the feather gently, and leaving
the Larde behind, and have snayles in a readiness to give her immediately after
ye have applied the said devise. And for lack of Snayles, give her the
forementioned pyll of Lard, mingled with marrow and sugar, and set her in the
Sun, or by a fire, without feeding of her till one hour after noone. And if
she endure well to be by the fire, or in the Sun, let her alone, for the
heat is very good for her. After this, give her somewhat more than half a
gorge of a young pullet, or if ye can come by any Myce or Rats, nothing is
better. But let her not stand in the air or in the wind, except the weather
be fair and warm. At night when she has indewed well, give her four or five
cloves of Mace broken, and lapped up in a little cotton, or in the skin of a
Hen: and do so three or four days, saving the suppositorie or pellet
aforesaid, for it will serve twice well enough. And thus shall you skoure your
hawke thoroughly. Look well to it, that she cast not up the cloves of Mace, for
they be singular good for hawks in all respects, specially for all humors
that surcharge their heads, and generally for all Filanders and worms. And if
you mind to rid a Falcon clean of the Cray, and of the said disease: give her
meat steeped in Goats milk, or in other milk, and do so four or five days
together: for the said milk is very good against the Cray. In the book of
the Prince, there is another receyt, for this disease of the Cray or stone.
That is to wit: Take the gall of a Pigge of three weeks old, and convey it
into your hawks beak, so as she may take it and swallowe it down whole
without breaking, and take heed that she cast up none of it again.
Afterward, give her a little piece of the Pigges flesh, of the bigness of a
Bean, and let her stand emptiepanneld upon the same until night, setting her
in the Sun or by the fire. This medicine is very good for all birds of
pray that are encombred with the Craye or Stone. Nevertheless, if a
Gossehawke or a Sparrowhawke have that disease (so it be not too sore) give it
her nor more but once. But as for other hawks that are of stronger mettel, ye
may give it them thrice. And when evening is come, feed your hawke with a
pullet, or with mutton, or with small birds, and the next morning steep her
meat in Goats milk, or woman's milk, feeding her so three days together
with small gorges, and she shall be sound. And if you will not or can not use
the said Receypt, ye may take a little oil Olyf, and somewhat less Honnye,
and wette your hawks meat therewith, for it is good to help that disease.
Some put the said things into a Hens gut tide fast at both ends, because a
hawke will take it the better: and naturally she likes not oil with her meat.

Master Michelin sets down another medicine which is this. Take Larde, marow
of beef, Sugar clarified and once boiled, and Saffron in powder, or each a
like quantity, provided that the Lard be first stieped in vinegar four and
twenty hours, and the water shifted three or four times, and set abroad in
the open air. Of the which things confected together, ye must make pylles of
the bygnesse of a Bean, whereof you shall give your hawke one or two, setting
her in the Sun or by the fire, and feeding her with poultrie or with mutton,
allowing her but reasonable gorges, four or five days together, and giving her
Maces as before: for they can not but do the hawke great pleasure in every
condition and part. Master Michelin teaches another receipte for this disease,
specially for Gossehawkes and Sparowhawkes, which I have tried often.

Cut a Sheep heart in small pieces, and when ye have let it lie stieping all
night in Asses milk, Gotes milk, or woman's milk, put a little boylde Sugar
into the milk, and gorge your hawke reasonably therewith three days together.
And assure your self, that this medicine is very excellent for the Cray, and
without danger for all manner of hawks. M. Martine says in auouchment of this
matter, that when a hawke cannot well mute with her ease, it betokens and
plainly shows that she has the stone Cray. For remedy whereof, take the
heart of a Hog, and a quantity of his sewet minced very small, and make them
into powder together, and give it the hawke in her meat three days
successively.

Again, I have seen some take the white of an egg, or the whole egg, with a
little Saffron in powder, well coyled and beaten together, which being bestowed
upon the hawks meat, has cured her.

Mallopin sets down yet one other receipt more. Put the juice of (water
Cressyes) in a Hens gut of one inche long, tied at both ends, and force your
hawk to receive it. Which done, set her in the Sun or by the fire, and feed
her not till noone, at which time give her but half a gorge of hot meat,
because of the medicine, which has set al her body out of temper. Let this he
done two or three days, and if you find the medicine to have scowred and taken
much at the first of your hawke, give her less and less, and so shall she
recover. The book of Princes sets down yet one other remedy for the same
disease, that ye might put it in ure which soever liked you best. Take a
penny weight of Persley seed, as much of Smallage seed, a dram of boylde
Sugar, a penny weight of Stauesaker, of Wheaten branne one dram, and half the
shell of an egg. Put them altogether into a good large psnet full of water,
and seethe it till it be consumed to the one half, and then streine it through
a cloth. Then take of Cassia Fistula one dram, and of Turbith one penny
weight, of Hermodactils two pennye weight, and of Aloes Cicotrine three penny
weight. Beat all these into fine powder, and put them into the water, wherein
the other mixture was boiled, and make thereof a Clyster in the bladder of a
Pygge.

Then take a great quyll of a Goose or of some other bird, and thereof make
the neck of your Clysterbagge fast tied to the bag, that nothing may issue
out of it, and so give your hawke the Clyster, as you have seen it given to
men at their need. This done, set your hawke in the Sun or by the fire, and
keep her empty till noone, at which time give her a pullets leg, and so she
shall recover no doubt.

For the disease called the Filanders which happen in the bodies of Hawks: and
first of such as are in their gorge.
THe chief Falconers say, that all hawks have the Filanders at al times, and
are never without them, like as it is said that no horse is without the Bottes.
There are four kinds of Filanders, and one other kind of Filanders, of which
I will speak hereafter in their due places. And with all these sorts of
Filanders, some hawks are more pestered than other some. The cause of them is
either their feeding on gross and foul meats, which engender and increase
those Fila^ders in them, or else for that inflying either ye field, or the
river, they break some small veins within their bodies, at the encounter, by
seazing too violently upon their pray. By reason whereof the blood bursteth
out into their bowels, and there dries and clottereth, whereof breed the said
Filanders in great abundance. Afterward by reason of the stinch of the said
blood so clottered and bakte, being corrupted and putrified in the bulk
because it is out of the proper vessels and veins where it ought to be, the
Filanders run about seeking the cleanest places of the body, to shun the
said noysyme stinche, and creep up either into the hawks heart, or into her
gorge, so as she dies of it. Again, some men say, that their hawks dye of
the diseases of the head, or of the Craye, when in deed they dye of the
Filanders or (which is worsse) of the ( Aignilles) a kind of Filanders for
which we want an english term. I will speak first of those Filanders that
craule up to the hawks gorges, and from thence to the holes in their palates
whereat the hawks do breathe, and by them into their brains, whereby they be
in danger of death. Ye may perceive this inconvenience in the gorge by this,
that when you have fed your hawke, the Filanders feeling the sweetness and
taste of the flesh, do stir and craule about in such wise, as you shall see
your hawke oftentimes gape. By reason whereof it comes to pass that now and
then she casts her gorge. Again, ye may know by this, that your hawke will
be strayning at them with her talons. Therefore cast her gently, and look into
her throat, and you shall see them crawling there. To kill the said
Filanders, master Ame Cassyan says thus: take a great Radysh root, and make a
hole in it, and fill it with water, and set it in embers very hot, putting
fresh embers to it continually by the space of half an hour or more, till it
be thoroughly well boiled, and as your water diminishes, fill it always up
again, howebeit that the Radishe yields water enough of his own nature.
Then put the Radishe into a dyshe, stamp it and press out all the juice quite
and clean. This done, put the quantity of a Peaze of Saffron made into powder
into the said water, and wash your hawks meat therewith when ye feed her,
and give her but half a gorge. And if she will not feed on it, let her be
kept empty, till she be very greedy and eger: do thus to her three or four
days together, and you shall kill the Filanders, and make your hawke sound.

Of the Filanders that are in hawks bowels, and in their reins.
YE shall discern that the Filanders are in her bowelles and guts, by her
heavy cheer and playnt in the night, for she will cry, and make a mournful
noise. Also ye may perceive it by this, that when ye take her on your fist in
the morning, she will stretch her self more strongly than she is wont to do of
ordinary, and sometimes she will make as though she would iouke upon your
si^te, and she will be busy with her beak about her back right over against
her raynes. When ye see these signs, assure your self that either the
Filanders or the ( Aigailles) do trouble her: and if she have not help of
them betimes, they will kill her, for I have seen many dye of that disease.
Master Ame Cassian gives this remedy for that mischief.

Take Lentilles, of the reddest that you can find and parche them at the fire,
and make fine powder of them, with the powder of Wormeseede, less by one half
than of the powder of Lentils, and mingle and temper them well together, and
make thereof a plaster, driven (upon cloth or leather.) Then deplume your
hawke in the place where her grief is, and lay the plaster to her panell,
changing it every day, for four or five days space together, and she shall
be cured.

If ye like not that receipt, Master Michelin gives you another, which is
this. Take the leaves of a Peach tree, of Rue, and of Wormeseede, and of those
three being brayed together, streyne out the juice, and afterward take then
powder of Wormwood, and put it into the juice, and lay it upon your hawks
reins playsterwise twice a day, evening and morning, for four or five days
together, and it will kill the Filanders, and save your hawke.

Master Ame Cassian tells yet one remedy more. Take (says he) a cloue of
Garlyke pylled, and give it your hawke in a hens skin, and it will heal her.

Of the Filanders or worms that are in hawks legs and thighs, which the
Frenchmen call Verse.
THere is another manner of Filander called the ( Verse) which comes sometimes
upon hawks that are lately taken, by setting them upon a pearche vnhooded or
vnseled, for they fall to beating of them selves with so great force, that they
break the veins of their legs. And this happens specially rather to Hagard
hawks, than to soar hawks. By means whereof the blood of those veins so
broken, pours and distilleth along their legs and pannels, between the
skin and the flesh, and there lying in lumpes, does convert to worms,
whereof the hawke dies. This disease may come also by her bating overmuch upon
the fiste, where through she bruseth her self violently: and sometimes he that
bears her furthereth it by his rashness and impaciencie. And ye may perceive
that ye Filanders and worms are in your hawks legs or bowels, by this: They
plume themselves oftentimes, yea, and the pendant feathers of their thighs and
of their panels, fall off voluntarily. Master Mallopin says, that the remedy
for this disease, is to wash your hawks thighs and belly twice a day, for
four or five days together, with the foresaid medicine of the leaves of the
Peachtree, of Rue, and or wormeseede, and with the wormeseede it self.

For the disease called in french the (Aiguils) an evil worse than the
Filanders, for which I know no apt English term, and therefore must borow the
french term of mine Author.
THere are found a kind of Filanders which are called ( Aiguilles) because they
be sharp like a needle, shorter and more perilous than are the great
Filanders, for as much as in seeking the cleanest parts of the body, to shun
the stinch and filth, they pearce the bowels and creep up to the heart, so that
your hawke perishes of them, if she be not regarded in time. Ye shall perceive
this disease by her shrinking and snyting upon the lure, as also by her
grasping with her foot more strongly in the mornings than she was wont to do,
and again by the often picking and beaking in her braile feathers, and near
her tuell. M. Mallopin gives this remedy following. Take Stauesaker beaten
into powder, the herb of Barbary, otherwise called in greek Pestora, and
Aloes Citotrine, of each a like quantity, coyled altogether into powder, and
give your hawke the quantity of a Bean thereof, lapped up in some part of a
Hens skin, or in cotton. Which done, set her in the Sun or by the fire,
and at noone allow her but half a gorge. You may give her of this powder
three or four days, so she be not too low already: for if she be not
somewhat high in flesh and in life, she will not be able to bear and brook
it. And if this medicine cure her not, take this that follows, which is of
master Mallopins device also. Burn Hearts horn well raked in the embers, and
when it is waxen cold, beat it into powder. Then take ye like quantity of
Lupins made into powder, as ye had of ye hearts horn, and as much again of the
powder of Wormseed, as of both ye other, and half as much Aloes Cicotrine, as
of the Hearts horn, and half as much Tryacle as of Aloes. Mingle al these
together with Honny by little and little, and force it to that thickness, yt ye
may make balls of it to the bigness of a nut, whereof ye shall give your
hawke every day one, by the space of five or six days, allowing her but half a
gorge after it. And if your hawke cast it again, let it be lapped in a little
cotto^ or in a hens skin, yt she feel not ye bitter taste of it. M. Ame Cassian
gives yet another remedy, which is the medicine made heretofore for the
Filanders, that is to wit, Rue and Wormwood, of each alike, and as much of the
Peachtree leaves, as of the^ both, with a little powder of Wormseede infused in
the juice of the said herbs. Then fill a Hens gut of an inche long, therewith
tied fast at both ends, and give it to your hawke.

You may use any of all these at your own discretion and pleasure.

When a hawke gapeth inordinatly upon the fiste of her keeper.
A Hawke will now and then sall to gaping, either upon her keepers fist, or
upon the pearche, and specially when she is set in the Sun being somewhat
hot. And this gaping may be understood and construed two ways. The one is
when she does it of her own nature, but that is not so often as the other which
comes by mischance, and that either of cold that she has taken, or of some
moist humor that destilleth down into her gorge. Some are of opinion that the
hawke which uses it often, is diseased with the Filanders, which creep up and
down in her gorge before she be fed, or after she has indewed, as I have
declared in the chapter of Filanders of the gorge.

The remedy by Master Ame Cassians aduyce, is to take the powder of
Wormeseede and of Wormewoode, of each alyke, and one quarter less of Aloes
Cicotrine, and of these three pouders mingled together, to give your hawke the
mountenance of a Bean in her casting, lapped up in a Hens skyn, or in any
such like device.

Of Apostumes that breed in Hawks.
WHen a hawke has any Apostume in her body, ye shall know it by the stuffing
of her nares, and by her inordinate panting, which accident comes sometimes
by rushing rashely into bushes, or by bating too much upon the pearche, and
moreover by over free encounter with her pray Now when she is so bruised and
chafed, and takes cold upon it, Apostumes breed thereby, because the
mischief is not known and looked unto before hand, Mallopin in his book of
the Prince gives this remedy. Take the white of an egg well beaten, and the
juice of Colewortes well broosed, as much of the one as of the other, and give
it your Hawke in the morning, in the small gut of a hen, and set her by a
fire, or in the Sun, and at noone feed her with mutton, or with a Pullet. The
next day take Rosemarie dried, and beaten into fine powder, and bestow it upon
her meat reasonably. For other three days give her Sugar, and three days
next after that, plie her again with her said powder, letting her stand
warm day and night, and feeding her with good meat: and the likelyhoode is
great she shall be recovered.

Of a Hawke that has her Liver inflamed.
THe inflammation of the Liver happens sometimes through the negligence of
such as have the keeping of Hawks. For they feed them with gross and
naughty flesh, such as is stale and stinking, without making of it clean, by
means whereof proceeds the said inflammation of the Liver. Also it happens
for want of bathing when need is, and for lack of water, which they ought to
have, or with ouerfleeing of them, when they be empty panneld. Ye shall
perceive this disease by their feet: for they will be chafed, and the color
of their chappes will be changed, and look whitishe through the heat of
their Liver. And if ye find her tongue scorched and scalded black, it is a
sign of death. To remedy this mischief, make the medicine of Snayles steeped
in Asses milk, or Gotes milk mentioned in the Chapters of the diseases of the
head, and of the stone: and give your Hawks of it three or four days in the
mornings. And if ye cannot get that medicine, ye may use the other that is made
of Lard, Marrow of beef, and of boiled Sugar, with a little Saffron, four or
five days together every morning, as is said before. For the scouring of her
will rid and abate her heat. And all that while for seven or eight days,
feed her with poultrie, or with mutton, steeped in milk: for milk is very
good for the heat of the Liver. And you must beware you feed her not with
Pigeon, nor with other gross flesh, for breeding her to inordinate heat.
Master Ame Cassian says yet further, that to allay the said heat in Hawks,
it is very good to steep or wet their meat in Endive, or Nightshade water:
and likewise in white whey newly and lately made. And that this manner of
feeding must be continued four or five days, till the Hawke be well scoured.
And that if the hawke have a desire to bowze the said whey, ye must let her
take her fill. Master Ame Cassian says moreover, that when your medicine has
scoured your hawke after the manner, and her tongue is not aught in the better
tune, ye must take oil Olife washed in two or three waters, and bath her tung
and throat therewith with a feather, three or four times in the day, for four
or five days together, and gently scrape her tung and throat with a devise of
silver, or of other metal for that purpose. And although she neither can
feed nor will, yet she must not be given over, but have meat gently conveyed
into her gorge by small morsels, and thrust down low enough with a fine
stick, that she may take it: for she cannot swallowe her meat by reason of
the swelling of her tung, and therefore she must be assisted in manner
aforesaid. Master Michelin says furthermore, that to comfort and strengthen
the Liver withal, ye must steep Rubarb in a dishfull of cold water all
night, and the next morning wash her meat therewith, continuing so by the
space of four or five days together.

Martin gives yet one other medicine, which is this: Take a Pullets gut,
thrice the length of your little finger, and cutting it in three pieces (which
you must tie fast at either end) fill them with Oil of Almonds, or oil Olife,
and thrust them into her throat so as she swallow them one after another: and
within an hour after gorge her reasonably with a young Pullet. And the next
morning take the seeds of Rushes, and the scrapings of Iuorie, and the dung of
Sparrowes, of each two penny weight, and make thereof a powder, and season your
Hawks meat therewith, and it will pleasure her. And these two medicines are to
be used when a hawke is not very foul within, for if she be, then are the
other former medicines better.

Of the Canker which breeds of over great heat in the Liver.
IF a Canker happen in the throat or tongue of a Hawke, by means of the heat
of the Liver: Make her the foresaid medicine of Snayles, or that of Larde, as
it is devised before, gyuing her her meat sowst in Milk, or Oil of sweet
Almondes, or Oil Olife: and let the Canker be washed twice or thrice a day,
till it wexe white and ripe. Then with your Instrument scrape it clean away,
that nothing of it remain. And if there happen to be any dead flesh in it,
put powder of Alome, or the juice of Lymons in it, and plye it still with the
said Milk or Oils, till she be thoroughly recured. And to make new flesh
grow up again, put to it a little honey of Roses.

Of the Pantas of the gorge.
Because many menned speak of the disease of the Pantas dyuersely, and yet
know not what it is: I will show three manner of Pantases wherewith Hawks are
diseased. The first in the gorge, the second comes of cold, and the third
is in the reins and kidneys. The first kind of Pantas comes of bating upon
the perch, or upon the fyst of him that bears them, by means whereof some
little veins of the Liver break, and the blood pours out upon the Liver,
which dries and cloddeth into small flakes, and those rising up afterward
when the Hawke bateth again, do stop the passages and windepype, and
thereof comes the Pantas. And sometime when the Hawke bateth, those flakes
do so ascend into her throat, and lie overthwart it, that she presently
falls down dead: insomuche that some hold opinion it is the Pantas that
makes Hawks to dye suddenly. For proof whereof, open your hawke when she is
dead, and ye shall find this malady in her throat. Master Ame Cassian
gives no medicine to this Pantas in the gorge, because it cannot be ministered
to her, neither by her throat nor otherwise. For the disease holds her in the
very windepipe, whereby the breath passes in and out. Nevertheless his
counsel is to cloze up the Hawke in some convenient Chamber, with Lattis
windows, so as she may not get out, and to set her two or three perches, that
she may flee from one to another, and have the Sun shine in upon her if it
be possible, and she must have always water by her. And when ye feed her,
her meat must be cut in small pellets, and have neither feather nor bone in
it, least she streyne hirselfe in tyring, and she must have but half a gorge
at once, and but once in a day. This is his counsel and advice for this
mischief.

Of an other Pantas that comes of cold.
AN other Pantas comes of cold when Hawks take wet in fleeing the field,
or the River, and are not weathered afterward, nor set in a place where they
may stand warm. Again this disease comes by standing where smoke or dust
may annoy them. And ye shall know the Pantas, by your Hawks panting, for that
she cannot draw her breath as she should do. Mallopin says that to remedy
this disease, ye must take the filings of iron, and meal of Lentils, of each
like quantity, which you must temper together with honey till it be like
paaste, that ye may make it in little balls as big as a peaze. Whereof you
shall give your Hawke two or three every morning, and after noone feed her
with good meat, but not with beef: which diet you shall allow her three or
four days. And if she amend not, season her meat with powder of Orpiment
two or three days.

Master Michelin gives this medicine following: Take Maydenheare, which
grows lightly at the heads of pondes and pittes, and therewithal the roots
of Persley, the roots of Smallage. Of all these take like portions, and boil
them in a good big pot that is new. Then streyne the water through a
Colander, and put thereto a quantity of clarified Sugar, with a little marrow
of Beef, and stir them all together, and thereof give your Hawke a portion
in the morning, and as much at Even, with a small tunnel, or otherwise with a
spoone or other fit device four or five days together, and let her not
feed till noone be past, then give her of Pullettes flesh without blood,
dipped in Oil of sweet Almondes, or Oil Olife washed in two or three waters:
and when ye have so sowst her meat, let it also be seasoned with a little
Saffron and Sugar. And four or five days after, if need be, give her on her
meat the powder of Orpiment without Oil other three or four days. And
afterward ye may return again to your oil of Almondes, or oil Olife, till
your Hawke be thoroughly recured.

Of the Pantas that is in the reins and kidneys.
IT happens sometimes that when a Hawke has been recovered of some great
grief by good keeping and intendance, afterward she waxes evil again, and
falls to panting, whereof breeds this disease of the reins and kidneys, in
manner of a Canker, as big as a bean, which swells bigger and bigger, in
such wise, that she falls to casting some part of her meat. This Pantas
differs much from both the other: for it will leave her seven or eight
days, and then vex her again more strongly. And somtymes it will take her but
from Month to Month, so as she shall bear it out a whole year. It is
discerned by this, that when she pants, she stirs her reins more than
her Pinions, whereas in the other she styrreth more her Pinions, than her
reins. Again to know it truly when it comes and goes every seven or
eight days, if your Hawke chance to die, rippe her and you shall find a
knubbe of the bigness of a small Bean full upon the reins and the small of
her back, whereof that disease proceeds.

Master Cassian gives this remedy for it. Ye must take the roots of
Capers, of Fenell, of Smallage, and of Parsley, and boil them together in a
new Pot, to the consumption of a third part. Then take an old Tyle, the
older the better, and make powder thereof. When you serve your Hawke, feed her
with flesh stieped in the water of the said roots, a quarter of an hour or
thereaboutes aforehande. In the morning when ye give her of that washed meat,
give her none of the powder, and at night wash not her meat with the said
water, but besprinckle it with the powder, so competently as she may receive
it, and give her not at any time above half a gorge: let this be done nine or
ten days, or more. If ye see that your Hawke amend not, continue it still:
for then was the disease very much confirmed, and the Hawke had borne it out
long, and it is hard to be cured. But if ye take the disease when it is new
and green, plye her diligently with this Medicine, and it will help her.

Of the Hawke that is morfounded by some mischaunce.
Morfound is the French word which does signify on English the taking of
cold.
SOmetimes it falls out that Hawks are morfounded by some mischaunce, and
sometimes also by giving them to great a gorge, specially when they be wette.
For then they cannot indewe nor put over their gorges, and so they surfeyte,
because their meat conuerteth into slyme, and gross humors which overthrow
their appetite, by means whereof they come oftentimes to their bane. Ye shall
know the disease by this, that if ye give your Hawke a great gorge, specially
over night, the next morning she will have no list to her breakefast, but
becomes cold, and so falls into great disease. Mallopin in his book of
the Prince, gives this medicine for it. When ye perceive her to be so
morfounded, and to have lost her appetite, give her no meat that day, but set
water before her, and let her bowze or bathe at her pleasure. When she has
bathed and is weathered again, throw her a live Pigeon, and let her kill it,
and take as much of the blood of it as she lysteth, but let her not eat past
one of the legs at that time. Afterward set her down on some high thing,
with water by her, and beware of giving her any great gorge. Then for four or
five days together give her five or six cloves of Maces, lapped up in a
hens skin, and that will recover her.

Of the disease that is called the privy and hidden evil in a Hawke, for
which we have no special term.
Sometimes Hawks perish for want of knowledge of some secret disease that
happens to them, and therefore I will tell you how you shall know it. The
Hawke that has that disease is always greedy to feed, insomuch that when ye
have given her a great gorge in the morning, she will have indewed it out of
hand, and if ye give her another at Noone, she will put it over by and by,
and if ye give her the third at night, she will dispatch that quickly also,
and the more she feeds, the more greedy and nippie she is. This disease
comes of this, that when your Hawke is very poor and low in state, and you
desirous to set her up quickly, thinking to bring her in good plight with great
gorges, you feed her with Pigeons and other flesh, which she cannot indew, by
reason of her poverty and weakness, for want of heat in the liver: the heat
whereof is the cause of all kindly digestio^ and indewing. Also you may know
this disease by her often muting which is waterish and thin, and besides she
does slise further tha^ she is wot to do, by reason of spedy induing her meat.

Master Mallopin in his book of the Prince, says that for remedy of this
disease, ye must stiepe a sheep heart cut in small pieces all one night
in Asses milk or Goats milk, and the next day give your hawke a quarter of
it in the morning for her beaching, as much at noone, and the rest at night,
forcing her to receive as much of the milk as ye can, and continue it five or
six days together, till ye see her mute kindly. Then feed her reasonably
with good meat, stieped in Oil of sweet Almondes, continuing it for three or
four days space twice a day. And as ye find your Hawke to mend, so increase
her meals by little and little, till she be in as good plight as she was
before, always continuing the said Milk: for some are of opinion that milk
is good for all diseases of a Hawke. Master Ame Cassian says, that to remedy
this disease, ye must take a Tortoys of the land, and not a water Tortoys, and
stiepe the flesh of it in woman's milk, Asses milk, or Goats milk, and give
your Hawke a quantity of it for a beaching three or four times, and a little
more at her feeding times six or seven days together. Afterward feed her
with sheep hearts stieped in Woman's milk, by little and little at once,
till she be recovered: and let her not stand in a dampishe or moist place,
but in warm places in the Winter, and in cool places in Summer, and always
hooded.

Of the disease and weakness in the reins.
WHen your Hawke cannot iumpe the length of her lines and Criance to your fist,
or from your fist up to the perch, nor bate with her wings: Ye may well think
that she has the disease of the reins. Therefore master Cassian wills you
to chop a Hares skin hair and all in very small and fine pieces, and to
mingle it with Cattes flesh, and to feed your Hawke with it seven or eight
days together: and if she indew it, she shall recover of her disease.

Of Havvkes that have the ague or fever.
TO know whither your Hawke have the Ague: Mark whether her feet be more
swollen than they were wont to be or no: if they be, then has she the
Ague. To remedy this mischief, Michelin says, you must mingle Arsenicke
and Capons grease together well sprinkled with Vinegar, whereof you must make
a little Ball, which you must cause your Hawke to take by casting her, and
use it in such wise as she may keep it, and it will rid the Ague.

Of the Havvke that voydeth worms.
IF a Hawke void Worms, by master Martins advice ye must make this
Medicine following. Take of the fine fylings of iron and strewe it upon your
Hawks meat, which (if you do well) must be Porke: and feed her so three or
four days with that kind of flesh so seasoned, and it will cure her.

Of the Teynte in a Hawks feather, and how many kinds of it there be.
HItherto ye have read of the inward diseases of Hawks: Now I will tell you
of the outward accidents: and first will speak of the Teynte, which the
French Falconers call Taigne, the Italians, Zignuole and Tarmae, whereof there
are three sorts. The first is when their principalles or long feathers begin
to drop off, by means whereof many Hawks are marred and cast away, without
knowledge how to help it. Master Ame Cassian says, that this comes
sometimes of the Liver, and of the excessive heat of the body, by means
whereof small pymples rise upon their wings, or on their traynes, which
afterward cause their feathers to drop off, and when they are gone, the
holes where they stood do cloze again, wherethrough the Hawke does perish,
if she be not remedyed. This disease is contagious, and one of them will take
it of an other, and therefore ye must not let the Hawke that is affected with
it, stand near a Hawke that is sound, neither must you touch or feed a
sound Hawke on the Glove whereon a sick Hawke has beende fed. And ye
shall know that she has that disease, by her often picking with her beak
upon her principal feathers of her wings and train, and by their dropping
away. Therefore cast your Hawke, and let her be well perused, and ye shall
find the said disease. For remedy whereof, Master Martin, and Master
Cassian say, you must cast your Hawke, and when you have sound the small pimple
whence the feather dropped first, you must get a little stick of Fur, which
is by nature gummie and fat, make a little pegge of it, not sharp at the
foreende, nor thruste it in with violence, but softly as you may. And if ye can
get none of that Woodde, then take a grain of Barlye, and cut of the
forepoynt of it, and annoint it with a little Triacle, or Oil Olife, and
convey it into the hole, so as it may stick a little out, and the hole not
cloze together, and stop again. Then with a small Lance or Penknife you
must slitte the pimple, and let out the red water which you shall find
there. After this take Aloes Cicotrine in powder, and put it into the gall of
an Ox, coyled in a dishe, and with those two mingled together, annoint the
slitte round about: and beware that there come nothing in the hole where the
feather grew, for it might do the Hawke great harm. This done, take of the
reddest Lentilles that you can come by, and less than half so much of the
fylings of iron, and mingle them together with Honey, and thereof make Pills
as big as a Peaze, and give your Hawke two or three of them every Morning.
Then set her by a fire, or in the Sun, and after Noone give her a reasonable
good gorge of a Pullet, or of Mutton, and if ye list ye may give her of
those Pills toward the Evening also. Let her meat be stieped in milk as
is said heretofore, five or six days together, and look always to the
incysions that ye made, and she shall be recovered.

An other remedy Martin gives for the same disease. Annoint the place with
some good Bawlme where the feathers fell away, and the Teynte will dye out of
hand, and fresh feathers supply the place again. And he says moreover,
that you must take the powder of Petre of Alexandria which is sold at the
Potecaryes, and mingle it with Vinegar, and annoint the diseased place with it
three or four times, and it will be whole.

The second kind of Teynte which fretteth the principals of a Hawke to the
very Quill, comes (as the chief Falconers affirm) of ill keeping, when
they have not their due intendance, being neither bathed nor scoured, nor kept
in wholesome places. And therefore we are forbid to keep a Hawke in a
sluttishe corner. Again, sometimes both in the mew and out of the mewe it
happens that by feeding them with filthy and loathsome flesh, they become
full of filth both within and without, whereof breeds such a sort of Worms,
as do utterly frette asunder and mar their feathers. The three chief
Falconers say, that for this second Teynt which fretteth the feathers of a
Hawke, in sort that they become like sticks, ye must take vine shredds, and
make thereof as strong Lie as ye can, and wash your Hawke once a day with it
thoroughly: and when she is weathered again, anoint all her feathers with
honey out of the comb. Then make powder of Sanguis Draconis, and Roch Alom
very small, and powder the foresaid Quils therewith, and your hawke shall be
recured. Or else take a Mole of that sort which breeds in Meadows, and put
her in a new earthen pot well covered and stopped, and set it on the fire one
whole day: which done, take her out again as she is, and make her into powder
very small. And when ye have bathed your Hawks feathers thoroughly with the
said Lee, by and by bestrow her feathers with the powder of the Mole a certain
time together, and it will pleasure her.

The third kind of Teynt is known in Hawks by the ryuing of their principal
feathers throughout along the upper side of the web of them. And that
happens commonly for want of clean feeding, and due attendance. To remedy
this, Mallopin says, that ye must take a green Reed, and cleave it all
along, and scrape out the pithe of it, and wring out of it as much juice
as ye can, wherewith you must moysten the said ryuen feathers all alongest
the ryftes of them, and they will close and shette again as before. And if
any feather happen to be dropped away, let a tente made of Fyrre or of a grain
of Barlye as is said heretofore be put into the hole of it, and use it after
the foresaid manner, and a new feather will grow again out of hand. If
this devise to cause a feather to grow again be not to your liking, you shall
in this collection find such as may content you.

Martin gives another Medicine and advice for the same, which is this: Take
twopennie weight of Orpiment, and nine graynes of Pepper, and make it into
powder, and cast it upon her meat, which must be reasonable warm. Again,
take three slices of Bacon, of that which is nearest the skin, and let them
be so small as your hawke may swallow them with ease: sauce them with a little
honey, and strew of the powder of the filings of iron upon the honey, and give
your hawke thereof three days together. After which time take a young Chicken,
and before ye boil it in wine, broose the breast of it, and open it with a
sharp knife or other instrument, that the blood may follow. Then feed her with
the said Pullets flesh warm, steeped in Goats milk, or other milk. Practice
this two or three days, and afterward thereupon give her good warm meat,
and she shall recover.

Of the Hawke that indeweth not, ne puts over as she should do.
IT falls out sometimes that a Hawke cannot well indew nor put over her meat
as she should do, and that is only because she is foul within, or has taken
some surfeyt, or else that when she was low and poor, her keeper being
desirous to set her up again to hashly, gave her to great gorges, which she by
reason of her weakness was not able to put over and indewe, wherethrough she
surfetted and forwent her appetite of feeding altogether. For remedy hereof,
Mallopin says ye must feed her with light meats, and little at once, as
with young Rattes and Mice, or with great Rattes, for there is more substance
in them than in the other, whereof ye must give her but half gorges, that
she may the sooner indewe them, and put them over. Or else feed her with
Chicken, or Mutton dipped in Goats milk, or otherwise. And for want of
those, take the yolke of an Egg, and give her a quarter of a gorge thereof,
and when ye feed her with the flesh of any live bird or foul, steep it well
in the blood of the same foul, and it will do her much good. So shall your
Hawke mount of her flesh apace: if besides that, you do also scour her with
pills made of Larde, Marow of beef, Sugar, and Saffron three mornings
together, and two hours after give her a reasonable gorge.

Another receyt which master Michelin gives, saying. When a Hawke indewes not
her meat, ne puts it over as she ought to do, but has surfeyted and
wants natural heat: Take very pure white wine, and steep her meat in it
luke warm, giving it her by little and little often times in the day, always
changing her meat, and see that it be light of digestion. Do thus to her
till she be in better tune again, giving her five or six cloves of Maces in
the Evening wrapped in a little cotton, or in some other such like device: for
that will warm her head, and all her other parts very well, and let the
Cotton be dipped in odoriferous old wine.

Of the Hawke that can neither cast up her meat, nor endew it as she ought.
ANother mischief is wont somtymes to light upon Hawks, namely that by taking
ouergreat gorges, they can neither indewe nor cast it again, and also that
many times a Hawke soareth away with her pray, and feeds so greedily upon it
by reason she was kept ouereager and sharp, that she can neither get it up nor
down, and thereupon falls in danger of death. Wherefore all men ought to use
discretion in feeding their hawks, that they ouergorge them not.

Master Cassian says that for remedy thereof, ye must set clean water in a
vessel before your Hawke, and let her bowze thereof at her pleasure. And if
she list not: then take the quantity of a Bean of Porke, of the fattest of
it, and two parts less of powder of Pepper, with a little salt brayed very
small, and when ye have mingled them all together, make a little ball of it as
big as a Bean, and put it in your Hawks beak, so as she may receive it.
Then set her by the fire, or in the Sun, and you shall see her cast her
gorge. But let not the Hawke be too poor, to whom you intend to allow this
pill, for if she be too low, she will hardly bear it.

Another medicine to make her cast her gorge.
RUb the roof of her mouth with a little Vinegar and Pepper, and she will cast
by and by. And if ye list, ye may also convey two or three drops of the same
into her nares, and it will make her cast her gorge out of hand. And if ye see
that the Vinegar and Pepper do vex and distemper her to much after the
casting of her gorge, spirt a little fresh water with your mouth into her
pallat and nares.

Of the Havvke that casts her gorge over much, and cannot indew as she
should do.
MAny times it comes to pass that when a Hawke has fed, she cannot keep
her meat but falls to casting, as soon as she has received it. This
comes of feeding her with gross, foul, and unwholesome meat not washed: or
else for that she is foul in the panell, and so cannot indew by reason of the
great store of filth that is within her. Therefore ye must beware that ye cut
not her meat with a foul knife, or with a knife that has cut Onions, Leeks,
Chibbols, or such other like things. To remedy this inconvenience, give not
your Hawke great gorges. And to bring her well in tune again, scour her with
the forementioned pills of Larde, Marrow, Sugar, and Saffron, by the space of
three days. And above all things, let not the Hawke that has cast her gorge
be fed a good space after it, but let her stand empty in the Sun with
water before her to bowse at her pleasure, for that will do her much good.
And when ye feed her, give her at the first time but a quarter of a gorge,
and at the second somewhat more, if she keep the first: and her meat must
be some live foul, and good of digestion, till she be brought again to her
natural plight. But if she keep not all her meat, give her young Rattes, or
live Mice: and for want of those, give her small birds till she be recovered.
If these foresaid things boot not, then by Mallopins advice, take Coriander
seed beaten in powder, and temper it with warm water, streyning it through a
cloth, wash your Hawks meat therewith four or five days together, and if
ye have no Coriander seed, take the juice of Coriander. And if your Hawke
mende not yet for all this: Follow the counsel of Master Michelins here
insuyng. Boil Baye leaves in white Wine till half the lyquor be wasted, and
then let it cool with the leaves still in it. Which done, force a Pigeon by
device to bowse so much of the Wine that she may die of it. Then immediately
feed your Hawke with the same Pigeon, and let her not eat more than a leg
of it.

Of the Havvke that has lost her appetite and will not feed, to make her
eager without bringing her lovv.
A Hawke does sometime lose her appetite of feeding by some misfortune, as by
taking to great gorges toward Evening, which she can not well indewe,
because the nyghtes are colder than the days. Also she may lose her appetyte
by being foul in the pannell, and diverse times by coldenesse, or by some
other disease which can not be perceived out of hand. For remedy whereof,
Master Mallopin says, ye must take Aloes Cicotrine, boylde Sugar, and
marrowe of Beef, of each alyke, saving that there must be least of the
Aloes, and when ye have mingled them together, and made them in little Balls
or Pills as big as Beans, give of them to your Hawke, and hold her in the
Sun till she have cast up the slyme and fylthe that is within her. And if
it happen to skoure downewarde, let it not trouble you, for it will do her
much good: and feed her not till noone, at which time give her good meat, and
serve her so three days together.

For the same disease, Michelin says you must take common Pills that are
given to purge men withal, and give one or two of them to your Hawke in the
morning, keeping her hooded by the fire, or in the Sun, looking to her that
she cast them not, (if it may be) to the intent they may cause her to scour
downward. And the book of the Prince says that it is good giving of those
Pills to Hawks in the beginning of September. For if they have the Filanders,
or any other inward disease, it scoureth them, and riddeth them of all
mischiefs that may ensewe. Three or four days after you have given your
Hawke these Pills, if she have yet no lust to feed, cast the filings of iron
upon her meat three or four days together.

Master Ame Cassiansayth, that for the same disease, when you perceive it, you
must give your Hawke a live Stockedone, allowing her leave to seize and tire
upon it, and to take the blood of it at her pleasure. And for want of a
Stockdone, small birds are good, and so be Rattes and Mice, so she have them
alive. And if you will have her to indewe them quickly, give her but half
gorges.

When Hawks are low brought, a remedy.
IT happens sometimes that Hawks are brought so low, that a man shall have
much a do to set them up again. And that comes through the fault of
unskillful keepers, rather than any other thing. For some give them ill meat,
cold and unwashed. Also Hawks do happen to grow poor by some disease that
their keepers are not ware of. Again sometimes it happens that a Hawke
soars away, and is lost four or five days, and so becommes poor for want
of pray. Master Cassian says, that if ye will set her up again, ye must feed
